WASHINGTON, Jan. 17.âThe House
"Committee on Elections of President,
Vice President and Representatives in
Congress gave a hearing to-day on bills
introduced by Representative Bourke
â¢ Cockran of New York. One provides
for publicity of contributions to cam
paign funds in Presidential elections
and the other for the appointmerit of a
.special commission to inquire into and
ascertain the amounts of money ex
pended by both political parties in all
Presidential elections from 1892 to 1904.
In advocating the publicity measure,
Cockran said there was the spectacle in
the last campaign of both candidates
engaged in recrimination on this sub
ject.- He contended that it should be
known who contributed and how much
was contributed, saying that publicity
would leave no ground for, wrongful
charges. It has been charged, he said,
that the large majority given the suc
cessful candidate was due to the use of
monpy, but he said he did not believe
that to be true. He said that when it
could not be understood why some one
has been appointed Embassador or
..given a high position, he was subjected
to the charge that he "came up hand
somely." The Embassador to Rome,
for instance, was to be given a wider
â¢diplomatic field, and he had heard it
stated, although he did not believe It
to be true, that it was because he had
made a large contribution.

1892. It was rumored, he added, that
$50,000,000 or $60,000,000 was used in one

LOBBYISTS
MUST WALK
CHALK LINE
Ooy. Polk of Missouri
Announces Strict
Regulations.
JEFFERSON CITY, Mo., Jan. 17.â
The stay of professional lobbyists In
Jefferson City will be limited during
the session of the Legislature. Gov
ernor Folk to-day enunciated rules
that all reputed lobbyists must follow.
The rules are simple enough and are
promulgated along the line of the Gov
ernor's Inaugural message. The Gov
ernor to-day advised certain railroad
corporation attorneys, who are admit
tedly lobbyists, that they must adhere
to the following rules:
1. On arrival In Jefferson City, or as
soon as possible thereafter, any profes
sional lobbyist must report his pres
ence In the city by presenting himself
at the Governor's office.
2. Such lobbyist must state to the
Governor the object of his visit.
3. A report must be made to news
paper represen'tatives, the same as that
made to the Governor.
4. A thirty-hour limit is placed on
the lobbyist's stay in the city.

FINDS ORE
AT THE RISK
OF HIS LIFE
Guide Takes Lucky
Tumble Down
Mountain,
Wbile Struggling to Save
Himsell He Uncovers
a Rich Ledge,
Manages to Get Samples of Rock,
Though Danger of Fatal Drop
Is Imminent
Â»
Special Dispatch to The Call.
LOS ANGELES, Jan. 17.âWhile
clinging for life to a manzanita bush
on the verge of a 500 foot precipice to
which he had fallen, and kicking des
perately for a hold in the crumbling
rock, Daniel E. Gump discovered a
ledge of copper ore which experts pro
nounce a bonanza.
Gump, who lives in Lordsburg, had
conducted a hunting party to Browns
Flats in the mountains, twenty miles
north of here.
Alone he essayed a short cut on the
return trip to show his companions it
could be done.
Instead of emerging on the trail, and
meeting the party where he had ex
pected, Gump suddenly found himself
on a perilously steep mountain,side. A
bowlder turned under him and he slid
and rolled down thirty yards, landing
cut and bruised in the brush on the
edge of an abyss. He was within an
inch of death. His shouts attracted
the other hunters after a long time,
and it took them nearly a day to res
cue him from the fearful dilemma.
When finally taken down with ropes
Gump had samples of ore which local
assayers find runs $50 per ton copper,
JlO gold and $3 silver. The ledge from
which he took it at the risk of his life
â¢measured several yards on the surface,
and capitalists have taken hold to push
extensive development. The strike is in
the forest reserve at the head of Live
Oak Canyon, where scores of prospec
tors have labored.
NEW ENGINE
MAY CHANGE
RAILROADING
Successful Trial ol
Monkey Motion
Pattern.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
OGDEN, Utah, Jan. 17.âA new type
of engine, known as the "monkey mo
tion" pattern, which, it is claimed, will
revolutiot.-ze steam locomotion on rail
roads, was successfully operated on
the Southern Pacific tracks to-day,
when a train of 1500 tons was run from
Ogden to Wadsworth, Nev., hauled by
one of the new engines.
The new type of engine was designed
by Edgar M. Luckett of the Southern
Pacific. Many advantages oyer the
standard type are claimed for the
"monkey motion." All the driving
mechanism is located on the sides of
the engine, making it easy of access.
The steam exhausts very rapidly and
there is no back pressure. â¢
It is estimated that the new engine
will save from 26 to 40 per cent in coal
consumption, being able to run fifty
four miles with one ton of coal, as
against twenty-five to twenty-eight
miles under the present system. The
standard engines now in use can be
changed to the "monkey motion" with
but very little cost.
Landslide in Norway Hits
Lake, Causing Wave Which
Drowns Fifty-Nine Persons
CHRISTIANIA, Norway, Jan. 17.â
Fifty-nine persons perished as the re
sult of an avalanche of rock at Naes
dal, north of Bergen, on Sunday.
A mass of rock was accidentally pre
cipitated into Loenvand Lake from the
neighboring hills, causing an immense
wave twenty feet high, which swept
the neighboring shores! Houses, peo
ple and cattle were swept away by the
rush of water, and it is known that
fifty-nine persons perished. Thus far
only four bodies have been recovered.
A great storm to-day stopped the re
lief work, as the surrounding district
Is unable to send help.
THE San Francisco CALL
Governor Sparks of Nevada
and Associates to Build
an Oasis in Death Valley
Special Dispatch to The Call.
AUSTIN, Texas, Jan. 17.âOn his re
cent visit to this section Governor
John Sparks of Nevada arranged for
the purchase of one hundred opossums.
The animals are to be captured by H.
L. Price and Captain John Tinier, and
are to be shipped by express to Reno,
Net. Governor Sparks said that he
had also arranged for the purchase and
shipment to the same place of 100 Mis
souri opossums.
TheBe animals are to be placed in a
1000-acre park, which Governor Sparks
and a number of wealthy associates are
establishing in Death Valley. This
park is situated seventy-five miles
south of Ooldfleld. Governor Sparks
says that it is to be made one of the
most beautiful spots In the West. The
water from the springs is to be used
for irrigating the shrubbery and trees,
and the little oasis Is to be made a
thing of joy and beauty for those who
travel across the desert. Steps have
been taken, he said, to erect a hand- i
some clubhouse.
To Governor Sparks has been dele- |
gated the task of stocking the park,
with animals. He will place In it a
large number of antelope, deer, wild
turkeys, squirrels, opossums, raccoons I
and other animals. Various kinds of 1
fruit trees will also be planted around I
the springs.
Chamber of ' Commerce in Berlin J
Again Receives Notification of
Government's Sentiments.
BERLIN, Jan. 17.âForeign Secre
tary von Rlchthofen has again con- â¢
veyed to the American Chamber of
Commerce intimation of the Govern
ment's unwillingness to recognize a
foreign institution in Berlin under
that name.
Secretary yon Richthofen says that
the Government has no objection
whatever to an association of Ameri
can merchants devoted precisely to
the objects for which the present
American Chamber of Commerce was
organized, but that chambers of com
merce in Germany are Government
institutions, chartered by the state,
and have certain defined Government
privileges, which cannot be conferred
upon a foreign institution under Ger
man jurisdiction.
MEASURED
FOR COFFIN
TOO SOON
Young Woman Gives
Undertaker a
Scare.
Regains Consciousness as
She Is Being Pre
pared lor Burial.
For the Third Time She Triumphs
Over Physicians Who Pro
nounce Her Dead.
Special Cable to The Call and New York
Herald. Copyright, 1906, by the New York
Herald Publishing Company.
I
LONDON, Jan. 17.â "t only remem
ber kissing my husband and feeling I
was getting colder and colder. Every
thing is blank to my memory until
I knew I was being carried down
stairs. Then I fainted again."
Thus spoke Mrs. Holden, a young
woman of Sapton, a village in the
county of Lancashire, to The Call cor
respondent to-day in explanation of the
extraordinary circumstances under
which she was brought back to life
while being measured for her coffin by
an undertaker. She looked very pale
and feeble, but spoke cheerfully.
"I have been subject to fainting,"
she added, "ever since I was born, and
this is the third time they have put
me by as dead. When I was fifteen
years old I was laid out for three
days."
The undertaker, through whose in
strumentality Mrs. Holden was re
stored to life, was accompanied by an
assistant when he called to measure
the body. It was a creepy story the
undertaker told of how he saw the
body laid out, rigid in the freezing at
mosphere of a disused upstairs room
and covered only by a sheet. He was
leaning over the pallid form to take
the measurements for the- coffin when
he was startled at observing a twitch- '
ing of the eyelids.
With an exclamation of surprise he
placed his hand over the woman's
heart, but all was still, not the slight
est pulsation being discernible. The
next instant the twitching was re
peated in an even more pronounced
fashion. The undertaker applied fric
tion to the inanimate body. Slowly
and with trembling uncertainty an
arm was raised, the glassy eyes quiv
ered in ghostly fashion, and a hand,
still deathly cold, clutched at his own.
The grief-stricken husband was
called, restoratives were administered
and the woman was carried down
stairs. Mrs. Holdon went into repeat
ed swoons, but they gradually lessened
in duration, and to-day she was able to
converse.

Attitude of France
Angers Japanese.
Terse Warning to Be Given
CALL BUREAU. POST BUILDING, WASHINGTON, Jan. 17.âThe
State Department officials have given a hint as to the contents of Japan's
answer to Russia. It will defend China and charge Russia with trying
to drag China Into the war from dishonorable motives by sending troops
into Mongolia. It also will rap France for her alleged violation of neu
trality in aiding the Baltic fleet and in other ways. Japan practically
will serve notice that, after she is through with Russia, she will drive
France out of Indo-Chlna in retaliation for her assistance to the Russians.
That Is the interpretation which will be put upon the note by this Gov
ernment and, it is expected, by the powers of Europe. The Japanese reply
Is expected within a few day.

WOMAN SPY
IN SERVICE
OF RUSSIA
Czar's Minions Will
Be Stationed in
Los Angeles.
â¦â
Special Dispatch to Th* Call.
LOS ANGELES, Jan. 17.âExamina
tion of a pretentious dwelling under
construction at the intersection of Jef
ferson street and Western avenue, in
the southwest section of this city, has
brought to light a Russian war plan
which is connected with the Czar's
Government by "underground wires"
running directly to Moscow.
The plan Is none less than the estab
lishment in Los Angeles of a Russian
spy station, to be in charge of Madame
Joroumsky, an employe of the Russian
Government, who for the last year has
been In the service of the Csar on the
Pacific Coast, spending her time alter
nately in San Francisco and Los An
geles. The woman Is now en route to
Russiaâpresumably to report in person
at Moscow. In her absence her hus
band is quietly directing the buHding
of the headquarters and its equipment.
The building is a ten-room structure.
The site is, isolated, shut In by a grove
of tall eucalyptus trees. An immense
cellar has been excavated, and a high
stockade fence is included in the plans.
Only Russian workmen recently
brought from the interior of that coun
try and unable to speak a word of
English, are employed. Of these there
are a score. Investigation shows that
the house is to be fitted up with a com
plete telegraphic system; the great cel
lar is designed for privacy in meetings.
The activity of the Japanese popula
tion of the Pacific Coast with relation
to matters affecting the present war is
said to be the incentive for the building
of this headquarters. Local Japanese
are aware of the plans of the Czar and
are watching the progress of thfTbuild
ing on Jefferson street.

Special Dispatch to The Call.
Hay's Note Given
to Russian Em
bassador.
WASHINGTON. Jan. 17.âSecretary
Hay's reply to the Russian note al
leging violations of China's neutrality
was handed to Count Cassini, the Rus
sian Embassador, to-day. At the same
time copies of the note were dispatched
to all European powers which ap
proved of the agreement for the limi
tation of the zone of hostilities.
Wfiile the communication is couched
in the most polite language of diplo
macy, there is no mistaking it's mean
ing. It expresses confidence in the be
lief that the Chinese Government has
committed no breach of neutrality, but,
on the other hand, has exerted Itself
to maintain a strictly proper attitude,
and urges the Csar to adhere to the
agreement by which tne war zone was
limited.
Hay also addressed a note to China,
through the American legation in Pe
king, which has a double purpose. It
formally admonishes the Chinese Gov
ernment to maintain an attitude which
can give neither belligerent just ground
for complaint and follows this per
functory advice with an inquiry as to
the truth of the Russian charges that
China's neutrality has been violated.
This inquiry is made to give the Gov
ernment in Peking an opportunity to
reply to the Russian note. The Chinese
Government is afraid to answer direct
ly the Russian charges with a note to
St. Petersburg, but it is anxious to
strike at the Czar over Hay's should
ers, and Hay is quite willing that it
should.
Hay already knows that the Chinese
reply will be a vigorous denial of all
of the Russian charges. It will con
tend that in every possible way China
has preserved her own neutrality, but
will give dates and details of viola
tions of China's neutrality by Russia.
It will point out the dangers to the
peace of Europe which will result
from continued invasion of Chinese
territory by Russian troops.
Touching the charge that many Chi
nese are serving in the Japanese army
for pay, the Chinese Government, af
ter auestioning the truth of the alle
gation and pointing to the failure of
the Russians to find Chinese among
the Japanese killed In battle, will
raise the point that even If Chinese
did serve as Japanese soldiers, such
fact does not of itself constitute a vio
lation of international law respecting
neutrality. A neutral nation may
properly be required to prevent the
organization within its territory of hos
tile expeditions directed against a bel
ligerent, but It cannot prevent Its citi
zens individually casting their lot with
either one of the combatants.
The other charge in the Russian com
plaint, namely, the employment of
Japanese officers to drill Chinese troops
in modern tactics, will. It is believed,
be declared to be within the rights of
any power, and not a violation of the
rules of neutrality.
Hay will make China's reply public
and in this. way China will get her
side of the case before the world. It
will be Intimated that China's reply
is fully believed by this Government.
Russia to-day, through Count Cas
sini, expressed her thanks to the
American Government for Its prompt
action in calling China's attention to
the charges that her neutrality was be
ing violated.
ST. PETERSBURG. Jan. 17. â The
Government of the United States has
not lodged a protest with Russia
against General Mlstchenko's opera
tions west of the Llao River, as cur
rently reported. The Foreign Office
points out that the sphere of hostili
ties, as specifically defined by Russia
at the opening of the war, extended
from the line of the Mongolian frontier
to the point where It intersected the
Liu River, a tributary of tbe Liao
River, along the Liao River and along
the Liu River to the point where it
crossed the Yinkow-Shanhalkwan rail
road, thence along the railroad to the
Liao River, thence to the left bank and
to the mouth only of the latter. A
small section of the right bank is be
yond the sphere, and there is no ques
tion of General Mistchenko having en
tered neutral territory.

PRICE FIVE CENTS.
Special Dispatch to The CalL2
Japanese Give Out Data Showing
. ': Conditions in Port Arthur.
â¢
.Tenacity of Defense Revealed
Â» .
Special Dispatch to The Call.
: PORT ARTHUR, Jan. 17.âOfficial
figures given out by the Japanese do
not bear out the statements cabled fb
â¢British newspapers by correspondents
. who are seeking to discredit General
.'Stoessel. It Is definitely announced j
that the entire Russian force in and
around Port Arthur was originally i
35,000 men. There were actually sur
rendered 9000 soldiers and sailors, of 1
whom a large proportion were unfit for
service. To the remainder fell the task
of garrisoning more than forty forts, |
large and small, against the attacks
of an army of 100,000 men. In the hos
pitals of Port Arthur the Japanese
found 16,000 sick or wounded men. j
During the siege 58 per cent of the |
officers were killed, and c.-ut of the en- |
tire number of officers only 28 went
through the siege unscathed.
The most surprising information
given out by the Japanese is that at the
battle of Nanshan Hill, the first great ,
combat on Liaotung Peninsula, the j
Russians numbered only 4000 men, i
and of these 2000 were killed or wound
.cd before the Japanese won the posi- I
tion. The Japanese losses in the bat
tic of Nanshan Hill were estimated at i
5000 men, and it was reported at the !
time that the Russian force numbered j
25,000. From the figures now given out
an idea of the unpreparedness <rf the
Russians at the outbreak of the war
may be gained.
The fortress here could have held out
for some time longer but for the fire
of the Japanese eleven-Inch howitzers,
which destroyed the food depots, the
workshops and the ammunition stores
and made the repair of the defensive
works impossible.
The latest news received by the gar
rison from General Kuropatkln was in
the middle of October, when he said
he had done his best to break through
the Japanese lines for the purpose of
â succoring Pert Arthur, but that he
had not been successful.
It is difficult to understand how the
Russian infantry remained in the
. trenches behind the Chinese wall, in
which great breaches were made. The
traverses and bombproofs of the bat
tery positions on Eboshiyama. in the
rear of East Rihlung Mountain fort,
fort "H," and the new Banjusan and
Wantai Hill positions in the rear of
. the Pahlung Mountain forts presented
an awful scene. Not a single gun
escaped being damaged by the Japan
â¢ ese bombardments. In the crest of the
ridge great holes were torn and the
gun emplacements were converted into
heaps of debris. Dozens of guns were
smashed and hurled from their car
riages down the obverse side of the
hill by the shell fire. Between Wantai
Hill and "H" fort a battery of ten-Inch
howitzers had been emplaced, and ev
ery one of these guns was damaged,
while some of them were totally de
stroyed.
One gun was evidently being loaded.
A shell was in the carrier ready to be
put to the breach when a Japanese
500-pound shell severed the carrier
chain and exploded. The entire gun
squad was killed by the explosion
and the gun was torn from its position,
while all around were the mangled re
mains of the gunners.
The effect of this last bombardment
of the siege must have utterly de
moralized the garrison, for the damage
done proves to have been much great
er than any one on the Japanese side
imagined at the time. The garrison
made its last attempt at resistance on
Christmas day against the Japanese
infantry, which by a spirited rush
. captured Wantai Hill. At 2 o'clock on
"the following morning the Russians ex
ploded mines under the battery posi
tions on the crest of the big East
Keekwan fort. These positions, which
were well constructed of concrete, are
now an awful wreck, strewn with
broken guns and debris of all kinds.
Not a vestige of the former works is
left, and the entire crest of the hill, one
of the most powerful battery positions
of the fortress, is a complete wreck.

Paroled Russian Officers Begin Their

officers.

RAIDING COSSACKS ESCAPE
Jnpancsc Repulsed in an Attempt to
Entrap Mistchenko's Men.
ST. PETERSBURG, Jan. 17.âGen
eral Kuropatkln, telegraphing yester
day to Emperor Nicholas, reported an
attempt by a strong detachment of in
fantry, cavalry and artillery to cut off
the raiding column of General Mist
chenko's cavalry on January 14, as the
latter was about to retire northward.
The Japanese, under cover of a mist,
outflanked the Russians. A battle en
sued, the Russian artillery inflicting
heavy losses upon the Japanese at
short range and then retiring. The
Russian losses were five officers and
forty men killed or wounded.
General Kuropatkin also reported
the return of a Russian patrol after
blowing up the railroad and destroying
a mile of telegraph two miles north of
Tatchekiao.

MAY RACE AT NEW ORLEANS

No Racing This Season on Chi
cago Tracks.
NEW ORLEANS. Jan. 17.âAl
though no official announcement |
could be made, it was learned from an
authoritative source to-night that the
owners of New Orleans Jockey Club
track have decided to try the experi
ment of carrying on racing here from
one end of the year to the other.
This decision has been reached be
cause it is considered doubtful as to
whether racing will be held in Chi
cago this summer and Ne> Orleans is
considered the next best place to carry
on a full year's meeting.
A definite announcement in regard
to this latter will be made as soon as
Ed Corrlgan returns from Chicago.

Tobacco Growing in Missouri.
The farmers in the vicinity of Wes
ton and latan are engaged in tobacco
raising. The experiment has passed the
experimental stage, and a number of
warehouses have lately been built for
curing and storing tobacco. Ben Doran
of Weston has built two tobacco ware
houses, each two stories high. The
warehouses are 108x45 feet. John
O'Dale, vice president of the Weston
Bank, has built a tobacco warehouse.
The MeAdow brothers of latan have
built another warehouse. Doran raised
a good deal of tobacco last summer in
spite of unfavorable weather, and ri
cently sold his entire crop at 8 cents a
pound in the warehouse. Tobacco raised
near latan and Weston is said to be of
unusual quality. Next year many more
farmers will add- it to their crop.â

FIRM DENIAL
BY MITCHELL
On Floor of the Senate He
Answers Charges Brought
Against Him in Oregon
SCORES HIS ACCUSEBS
Makes Deep Impression on
Colleagues as He States
That He Is Without Guilt
WASHINGTON, Jan. 10.âThe unus
ual spectacle of a United States Senator
explaining on the floor of the chamber
charges made against himself by a
court of law in his own State was wit
nessed in the Senate to-day when
Mitchell of Oregon spoke of the* in
dictment recently returned against
him by an Oregon Grand Jury. The
Senator had not previously appeared
in, the Senate since the indictment was
returned and he was received by his
fellow Senators with many evidences
of good will. Jle was much affected,
his emotion at times being such that
he read his statement with great diffi
culty. In part, it was as follows:
I assert in the most positive and unqualified
manner that each and every one of these
charges, lit so far as they relate to or in
volve me, are absolutely, unqualifiedly and
atrociously false, and I here and now Indig
nantly and defiantly denounce authors
and each and every one of them, and brand
them publicly as malicious and atrocious liars.
. But I desire to be more specific, and there*
fore I further deny, in terms the most abso
lute and unqualified of which I am capable of
using, that I ever, either In the month of Jan
uary, 1902, In the State of Oregon or at any
other time or place, unlawfully or feloniously,
or otherwise, conspired with Blnger Herr
mann, then Commissioner of the Land Office,
and S. A. D. Puter. Horace H. O. McKinley,
D. W . Tarpley, Emma L. Watson, Salmon B.
Ormsby. Clark E. Loomis and William H. Da
vis, or with either or any of them, or with any
other person or persons, to defraud the United
States out of any part of its public lands lo
cated either in township 11 south, range 7
east, in the Bute of Oregon, or any other
public lands either in the State of Oregon or
elsewhere. Â«
1 /- rt i ier deny ln the mo>t absolute and un
qualified terms that 8. A. D. Puter did, either
in the city of Washington in March, 1902, or
at any other time or place, offer me, or pay
or Slve meânor did I on March 9. 1902, ln
Washington, D. C, or at any other time or
place, accept or receive from said 8. A. D.
Puter the sum of $2000 or any other amount
whatever as an Inducement to use my influ
ence with Blnger Herrmann, then Commis
sioner of the General Land Office.
Senator Mitchell then told in detail
how he had assisted Puter and Mrs.
Watson in Washington to secure the
early consideration of claims in
which they were interested. He con
tinued: j

the entries or any of them. I did understand

For what I did before the Land Office in

Then the Senator attacked Francis

In the prosecution of the land fraud

ing that subsequent to the convictions

cember and before sentence had been

The Senator referred to his long ca

until this time had any charge ever

as a man, member of the bar or mem

this time, but he declared. "I am an
innocent man and I have at this stage

any other Senator to come here in de

He trusted and believed, he said,

a thorough investigation of every one

ator said in conclusion:

FIND BODIES OF VICTIMS
AFTER LAPSE OF A WEEK
Two Elderly Women Accidentally Asphyxiated
For nearly a week two aged women
lay dead from gas asphyxiation in a
little cottage at 30 Lynch street. One
was Mrs. Mary Conway, mother or.
Frederick B. Conway, formerly clerk
in Judge Mogan's court, but now a
fugitive from justice. The other was
Mrs. J. D. McMurray, mother of
Policeman H. McMurray. The bodies
were discovered yesterday.
From ail indications death was ac
cidental. Mrs. McMurray visited Mrs.
Conway, who owns the house, intend
ing to stay during the absence of the
latter, who was to leave soon on a trip
to Arizona.
The two old ladies were seen by
some neighbors as they entered the
house on Wednesday night. Mrs. Con
way retired to her own room in front,
and her companion occupied a room in
the rear of the cottage. In extinguish
ing the gas the visitor evidently did
not thrn the cock far enough. The
gas fixtures were new and worked
rather stiffly.
Mrs. McMurray fell asleep, and the
room filled with gas. The poisonous
fumes rapidly permeated the entire
hcuse. Mrs. Conway was awakened
and evidently thought that the leak
was from the gas fixture in her own
room. She attempted to close the
stop cock but instead turned it on.
Then she staggered through the oth
er rooms, fumbling in the dark with
the gas jets in each. She realized that
one of the stop cocks was open and
was filling the place with gas. But be
wildered and unable to see she could
not discover which one.
OVERCOME BY THE FUMES.
As she reached about in the dark
the poisonous fumes gradually dead
ened her senses. She made one effort
to retrace her steps to her own room
where she must have heard the omin
ous hiss of escaping gas. Just as she
reached the door of her room she fell
and after a futile effort to rise lay still.
Every door and window in the house
was locked. The gas filled every nook
and corner of the rooms. The gasps of
the two old women grew fainter and
fainter, and soon the stillness of death
reigned in the little cottage.
Neighbors soon began to miss the fa
miliar figure of Mrs. Conway, but it
was whispered around that she had
left for Arizona to visit her sick daugh
ter. Mrs. McMurray was taking care
of the home, it was reported. But peo
ple notice that mail left at
the doorway of 30 Lynch street was not
being taken in. People In the neigh
borhood thought that there was some
thing wrong in the little cottage.
Mrs. J. Johnson, who lives in the
house adjoining, notified Mrs. Dwelle
a sister of Mrs. Conway, of the pecu
liar circumstances. George Dwelle, a
nephew of the old lady, immediately
went to the house. He knocked at the
door, but received no response.
He climbed to a window, and, peer
ing into his aunt's room, saw her lying
in the doorway. Dwelle notified the
North End Police Station, and Police
man O'Sulllvan hurried to the place.
He broke open a rear jdoor, but was
driven back by the rush* of gas. Five
jets were turned on.
MAKE GHASTLY DISCOVERY.
It was some time before the pair
could enter. They found Mrs. McMur
ray lying on a bed in the rear room,
and Mrs. Conway stretched across the
threshold of her room. Both had been
dead for nearly a week. The bodies
were removed to the Morgue.
Policeman McMurray was sent for
and identified one of the bodies as that
of his mother. Mrs, J. D. McMurray
was 67 years old. She had been living
at 227 Flood avenue.
Mrs. Conway was 68 years old. Her
son, Frederick H. Conway, jumped a
$500 bail bond several years ago, and
has not been heard from since. He was
implicated with Daniel Sullivan in the
forging of bail receipts. Sullivan is
now doing time at San Quentin.
Conway deserted his bondsmen just
TWO AGED WOMEN WHO WERE
ASPHYXIATED BY GAS, AND HOUSE
WHERE THE TRAGEDY OCCURRED.
WILL PROCEED
WITH CONTEST
Testimony to Be Taken at
Once .in Peabody Case in
Legislature of Colorado
DENVER, Jan. 17.âAt a joint ses
sion of the two houses of the Gen
eral Assembly this afternoon to take
action on the contest filed by James
H. Peabody for the office of Gov
ernor, the request of the attorneys for
Governor Adams for an extension un
til 2:30 o'clock on Saturday afternoon
of the time in which to submit an
answer made in the contest papers
was granted by a vote of 61 to 34.
By the same vote the Legislature
decided to proceed at once with the
taking of testimony in the contest.
On motion of Senator Parks a com
mittee of five was appointed by Lieu
tenant Governor McDonald, who pre
sided over the joint session, to draft
a set of rules and regulations which
shall govern the order of the contest.
The committee is composed of three
Republicans and two Democrats.
Attorney VValdron, appearing for
Governor Peabody, said he wished to
be understood as not opposing any
reasonable request by Governor
Adams. He argued, however, that the
pircumstances required no written
pleadings, and he was clearly of the
opinion that any extension of time
gained would result in delay and
prove fatal to the cause for which
the joint session had been called.
Both Governor Adams and his at
torneys protested vigorously against
immediate action by the Legislature,
asserting that the proceedings would
necessitate his presence and that of
his counsel and probably preclude a
proper and thorough investigation of
the charges filed against him and a
satisfactory answer thereto.

Special Dispatch to The Call.
CALL BUREAU. POST! BUILDING,
WASHINGTON. Jan. 17. -The bill ap
propriating $20,000 for th> beautifica
tion of the Presidio at San Francisco
passed the Senate to-day. Fifty thou
sand dollars was originally named ln
the bill, but Senator Perkins amended
it to conform to General Mac Arthur's
recommendation, which named $15,000
as the sum that could be employed in
the work this year. The appropriation
Is a continuing one until $50,000 is
reached. This appropriation is ex
pressly for the extension of walks,
drives and the adornment of the Pre
sidio, and not for military equipment.
Senator Bard to-day favorably re
ported the bill for the transfer of por
tions of Yosemite Park to the Sierra
forest reserve. He had boÂ«n requested
to delay the fVport by citizens of
Fresno, and promised to do so if they
wished to come to Washington and be
heard, but no wordfi having b3en re
ceived by them, the Dill was forwarded
at once. Senator Kittridge will report
the House bill to-morrow.
Immediately after the Senate con
vened to-day, Perkins presented and
had read a protest from the California
Legislature against Commissioner
Yerkes' recommendation of a tax of 25
cents a gallon on grape brandy. The
protest was referred to the Committee
on Finance.
Lodge of Massachusetts presented
the conference report on the Philippine
railroad and bond bill and explained
i the House amendments. Of these, he
I said, there were only two of impor-
I tance, one of which, he said, regulates
j the distribution of the earnings and
j profits of railroad companies and the
, other, provides for an appeal to the In
sular Supreme Court of suits at law in
which the Government may be inter-;
ested. The report was ordered pfinted.
SWAYNE'S FATE IN DOUBT.
House Will Vote To-Day on Question
of Impeachment.
WASHINGTON, Jan. 17.âWith an
agreement to vote on the Swayne Im
peachment articles to-morrow at 3:3-0
o'clock, the debate to-day was carried
on at high pressure for more than five
hours. Grosvenor furnished the text
for a very vigorpus speech by Bourke
Cockran by the reading of a letter
from Judge Pardee of New Orleans,
declaring that politics was at the bot
tom of the impeachment proceedings.
The fact of a Judge transmitting such
a letter, Cockran declared dramatic
ally, was "a monstrous spectacle."
Grosvenor asserted that there was
no ground presented for impeachment
in the report of the committee. The
other defenders of the Florida Judge
during the day were Lacey of lowa,
Nevin of Ohio, Moon of Pennsylvania
and Crumpacker of Indiana.
Lamar of Florida closed the debate
for the day, reviewing the sentiment
of his State and the record of Judge
Swayne. He declared there was am
ple ground for impeachment.

FRIENDS OF UNIVERSITY
FARM BILL WIN VICTORY
Commlt/ee Reports Against Buifding
4 / . , â  *
SACRAMENTO. Jan. 17.âThe fight
against Lukens' bill to appropriate
$250,000 for the construction of an agri
cultural building for the College of
Agriculture at Berkeley began this
. afternoon in the Senate Committee on
Agriculture and Dairying and it looks
. now as though the measure would be
.defeated. The committee, which is
composed of Lynch, Rush, Muenter,
Greenweli, Rambo, McKee and Diggs,
stands in favor of Diggs' bill, appro
priating $250,000 for the purchase of
an agricultural farm of not less than
320 acres and is advancing on the
theory that it would be strange reason
ing that would put the college build
ing one place and have the farm in
another.
After considerable discussion the
. committee agreed to report favorably
â¢ on the Diggs bill and ask that it pass,
leaving the question of the building for
further consideration. It is the sense
of tbe committee that the entire col
lege of agriculture, building and all,
should be transferred to the site of the
ffrm it recommends should be pur
chased. It points to the fact that oth
er branches of the university are do
ing splendid work far removed from
Berkeley and holds that none requires
such removal from metropolitan sur
roundings more than the college of
agriculture. A warm debate on the
floor is anticipated when the recom
mendations of the committee are read
to Ihe Senate in general session.
Southern California has a scheme
afoot to get the $50,000 it wants for an
experimental station by cutting In on
the proposed-appropriation for an agri
cultural <"ollege or standing out against
that appropriation unless it is allowed
the sum it desires.

It Is pointed out that the preliminary
work of reclaiming the inundated lands
of the Sacramento and San Joaquin ba
sins will cost much money before
means can be devised to make the re
claimed lands pay for their own sal
vation; that San Quentin prison must
be practically rebuilt; that Folsom
prison must be walled; that at least
two of the State Normal Schools must
be rebuilt; that additions must be
made to the hospitals for the insane,
all of them being overcrowded; that
the Capitol building must be repaired
at much expense and that much public
work must be cared for by increased
appropriation.
The preliminary step in the proposed
reorganization of the State's taxation
system was taken up to-night at a
conference of the Legislative Commit
tee of the Senate and Assembly with
Governor Pardee. There was a full
attendance of the committees and af
ter a lengthy discussion it was decided
to appoint a subcommittee of seven,
including the chairman of the Revenue
and Taxation Committee?. whose duty
it shall be to draw up a proposed con
stitutional amendment embodying th_
proposed chanpes.

AGREES WITH BELSHAW.
Drainage Committee Favors Bill Con
cerning State Land Titles.
SACRAMENTO, Jan. 17.âThe Sen
ate Committee on Drainage. Swamp
and Overflowed lands held a session
this afternoon. It agreed to report fa
vorably on Belshaw's bill for the ralief
of purchases of State lands by legal
izing applications heretofore made for
the purchase of such lands under the
erroneous belief that they were
swamp lands, when in fact they were
salt marsh and tide lands and vice
versa. Emmons, while joining in the
recommendation that the bill pass, re
served the right to protest against the
measure on the floor should It be
shown later that the bill would vali
date any titles that had been fraudu
lently procured.
Poisoning May Cause Death.
Warren J. Brown, of 1 Lexington
j avenue, was picked up by Patrolman
.W. F. Sheehan early this morning at
Third and Howard streets and booked
at the Southern Station on a charge
of drunkenness. He was taken sick
shortly after arrival and was sent to
the Central Emergency Hospital,
where he was treated for alcoholism.
Later he had a relapse and artificial
respiration was necessary. It is be
lieved he had alcoholic poisoning or
had been given knockout drops. De
tective O'Day is investigating the case.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, JANUARY 18, 1905.
PRIZE FIGHT BILL CAUSES A
LIVELY DISCUSSION.
Strict Investigation
of Fistic Game
Is Desired.
Special Dlsr.atch to The Call.
SACRAMENTO. Jan. 17.âAnother
assault on the treasury by would-be
junketers was met with a hall of
oratory and successfully repulsed ln
the Senate this morning. This second
effort was attempted after much more
careful preparation than marked the
one that met disaster yesterday, but
it fell the heavier because the more
heavily backed.
Considerable surprise was created
when, shortly after the Senate con
vened, Bauer of San Francisco intro
duced a resolution providing for a
junket of investigation to the south
ern institutions, the committee to be
composed of Coggins, McKee, Muen
ter, Haskins, Bunkers, French, Nelson,
Markey, Bauer, Welch, Irish, Selvage,
Wright and Sanford and an assistant
sergeant at arms, all of them northern
men. The word went around that this
committee was the result of a com
promise with the southerners by
which a committee composed of Ward,
Broughton, Carter, Pendleton, Em
mons, Lynch, Greenwell, Leeke, An
derson and Diggs, all southern men,
were to be given a trip to investigate
the northern institutions. It was a
good plan, it was thought, and would
insure the support of the north for
the scheme of the south and the sup
port of the south for the scheme of
the north. But the promoters of the
resolutions forgot that under the rules
it takes a two-thirds vote to authorize
a junketing tour and after securing
the promise of a majority to support
the measures they rested. This was
fatal to their cause.
MEASVRE IS OPPOSED.
The conclusion of the reading of
Bauer's resolution found Belshaw,
Shortridge, Leavitt, Selvage and An
derson, the two last named being
among those listed for the junket,
seeking recognition from the-President
to oppose the measure. They were each
given an opportunity in turn to voice
their views and all agreed that the
time has not yet arrived when junket
ing would be advisable and even when
it is advisable they agreed that the
committee as provided for was entire
ly too large and would practically strip
the Senate.
French sought to stop the debate by
moving the previous question, but he
was unsuccessful and the opponents of
Bauer's junketing question drove home
their points. It was then put to a
vote and secured the support of twen
ty-two. with thirteen against. Tfie
proponents of the measure looked over
joyed when the President pronounced
the resolution adopted, but their joy
was short lived. Simpson rose to a
point of order, however, and pointed
to the rule that called for a two-thirds
vote. President Anderson glanced at
the rule, said the point was well taken
and declared the resolution defeated.
JUNKETERS DEFEATED.
Bauer and his followers looked dum
founded and their confusion had not
left them when the resolution calling
for the committee to visit the north
ern institutions was put to a vote, with
the result that some of them voted
against the measure, defeating it by
even a greater number than that
against Bauer's resolution. Senator
Lynch, who voted against the last res
olution, when he intended to vote for
it, asked for a, reconsideration, but his
motion was quickly frowned down, and
the junketers were finally defeated.
Just what further action they will take
to carry through their plans Is a mat
ter of speculation.
A resolution calling for the appoint
ment of a special committee by the
chair to investigate conditions pre
vailing in Napa Hospital for the In
sane was next presented by Senator
Emmons and sent to committee. The
resolution follows:
Whereas, Recent occurrence* in the Napa In
sane Asylum have shown that a serious defect
exists In the manner of selecting custodians
of the insane; and whereas, the investigation
now going on In the courts will only serve to
determine the guilt or Innocence of the par
ties accused, and will not throw any light-upon
the manner of the selection of the guards thai
resulted In the accusation of the two now un
der investigation. Therefore be It
Resolved, That the Senate of the State of
California do investigate the said occurrence
by means of a special committee to be ap
pointed by the president of the Senate, and j
that s-aid Investigating committee be directed
to reaert to this Senate some plan that will
prevent the st-lectlon of such guards In the
luture to care for our Insane and helpless
wards.
It was again time for Bauer to seek
further favor at the hands of the Sen
ate, but he was again destined to dis
appointment. The papers, he said, had
been making considerable comment re
garding Senate bill 24, that has as its
object the doom of prize fighting in
California, and he wished to Investi
gate the claims of the newspapers,
which he said could be done by the
' adoption of the following resolution:
Whe.-tas. Prominent newspapers of the c:ty
I o." fc'an KranciEco have quoted many prominent
| clt'.zeng ii - expressing views on Senate bill
No. 24, ii >w p?ndinrj'before the Committee on
I l'ubllc in a:.-, and
i Whereas, it would be of great value to this
. committee in patslrrg on said Senate Bill No.
2-1 tj havj thy evidence and the testimony, oi
these parties: and
W liereat. It Is the desire of the commltt?.
to Inquire Into the methods of clubs giving
professional prize tights and to hear the testi
i mony of thÂ» directors of said clubs; be it
Resolved by the Senate of the State of Cali
fornia, That the chairman of the Committee
on Public Morals be and he is hereby given
the power to issue subpoenas for the purpose
of compelling attendance of necessary persons
and to send for books and papers necessary to
and for the better consideration of said Senate
bi.l No. 24.

Claim Is Made That
Extra Session Is
Needed.
Special Dispatch to Th* Call.
SACRAMENTO, Jan. 17.âTalk of an
extra session of the Legislature to!
pass the code revision bills is being 1
indulged in by some of the statesmen
here. The claim is made that there will
not be time at the present session to
attend to these bills. Already the State 1
has expended between $200,000 and $300,- j
000 in trying to have them adopted. The j
Supreme Court held that the action of j
the Legislature of four years ago m ,
adopting them as a whole was illegal, |
and that action was nullified. Two'
years ago their adoption singly was'
prevented by the Judiciary Committee, '
of which Grove L. Johnson was chair- j
man, and a constitutional amendment j
adopted at that session giving the i
Legislature power to adopt them as a
whole was defeated by the people.
There is nothing for the present Leg
islature to do if it wishes the tangle
cleared up, the dead matter eliminated
and the live bills got together in handy
form but to deal with each of them .
that has been changed even by the in- j
sertion of a comma, separately, and .
pass it in the same way an original bill !
is passed. There are about 250 of the
bills in which some slight changes,
some very trifling, have been made by
Code Commissioner Davis, who is at
work daily on the revision. Half of
them will be introduced in the Senate
and half in the House. About forty
of them have been introduced to date.
Opponents of an extra session claim
that all the bills can be disposed of at
three evening sessions.

FIGHT OVER JUDGES.
North and South Ready to Lock Horns
on Measures.
SACRAMENTO, Jan. 17.âThe Los
Angeles bill by which three new
Superior Court Judges are to be added
to the bench of that county will not
have the easy sailing in the Assembly
that it had in the Senate. The San
Ftancisco delegation thinks that in
view of the fact that San Francisco
pays one-quarter of the taxes of the
entire State, its bill to increase the
salaries of the Superior Judges of San
Francisco County should become a
law and It will ask the Los Angeles
delegation to stand for that law in re
turn for aid for its measure.
If the Los Angeles bench is increased
to nine members, that county will re
ceive $27,000 a year for its judges,
while San Francisco will, under the
present law, get but $36,000. the State
paying half the salaries of Superior
Judges. The San Francisco delegation
dres not think the proportion will be
fair.

ified me because if i have appointed

soon. However, I generally know the
men I appoint pretty well before I
name them for office."

Needs of the State's
Prisons Subject
of Report.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SACRAMENTO, Jan. 17.-r-In the
Assembly this morning the report of
the special hold-over Committee on
Prisons and Reforms, that consisted
of Prescott, McKenney and Dunbar,
the latter not now a member of the
Assembly, was taken up as the spe
cial order. In presenting a resolution
to adopt the report of the committee
McKenney said:
"This report was drawn up after a
visit by the committee to all the pris
ons, and we are indebted to every
country on the globe for information.
Secretary of State Hay of our own
country was especially obliging. We
have collected reports and other litera
ture on penal institutions and penal
work from all over the world. Some
of the signatures alone are valuable.
The material Itself is extremely so,
and it will make a nice collection for
the library." I
Four pages brought down several
big baskets of the reports and other
documents gathered by the committee
and they were passed over the desk to
Clerk Lloyd. On motion of Goodrich
they were ordered sent to the State
Printer, and the Librarian was in
structed to have them bound and add
ed to the department of criminology.
"The report filed by the hold-over
committee," said Speaker Prescott,
taking the iloor, "urges some radical
reforms and some complete changes
in our prison methods. It Is not the
Intention to commit this body in any
way as to the final disposition of
these bills through the adoption of
the report. It Is desired that the
committee adopt the resolution as an
indorsement of the work of the com
mittee up to this time, leaving the
bills to be discussed on their merits
and acted on as the Assembly deems
expedient. I wish to emphasize the
fact that we have presented here in
the documentary evidence we have
gathered suggestions from nearly
ever- State in the Union and nearly
every civilized country. Though we
have in California some of th% finest
penologists in the world, our prisons,
it is too bad to confess, are, because
of imperfect machinery, a disgrace to
the State."
REPORT IS ADOPTED.
The report was adopted and Mc-
Kenney introduced a resolution
granting permission to the present
Committee on Prisons and Reforms,
consisting of McKenney, Lynch, Olm
sted, Amerlge, Ells. Bliss. Boyle,
Becket and Wickersham, to visit the
State prisons and the reform schools
at Whittier and lone.
"Does the committee intend to pay
its own expenses?" asked Houser.
"It certainly does not," said Mc-
Kenney. ,
"That is a matter to be attended to
afterward," said Bliss.
Houser moved that the matter be
referred to the Committee on Contin
gent Expenses, but there was no sec
ond and the resolution was imme
diately adopted. It is the first resolu
tion of a junketing nature to be intro
duced In the lower house this session.
The imDetus given to prison reform
legislation by the report of the hold
over committee is not to be allowed to
weaken. While the House committee
on this branch of legislation was in
session yesterday, Governor Pardee
sent a message asking that a Joint
meeting of the Prison and Reform com
mittees of both houses, the State Board
of Charities arid Corrections, the
Board of Prison Directors and himself
be called. McKenney, Wickersham and
Olmsted were appointed a committee to
call on the Governor and arrange for
such a gathering.
A large public meeting to discuss the
recommendations made by the hold
over committee'and to go Into prison
and reformatory matters generally for
the benefit of the legislators will be
held in the Assembly chamber some
evening soon. The hold-over commit
tee has sent out invitations to various
State officials and many other prom
inent people throughout California to
attend. The date has not been fixed,
but the meeting will be held early in
the session.

WILL MEASURE
MOUNT WHITNEY
Survey to Secure Accurate
Figures on Highest and
Lowest Points in Country
A NOVEL UNDERTAKING
From the Peak Party Will
Go to Death Valley, Where
Is the Greatest Depression
SACRAMENTO, Jan. 17.â R. A.
Farmer, of the United States Geolog
ical Survey, left this city last evening
upon mission that may establish, in ac
cordance with scientific requirements,
the exact height of Mount Whitney
and also the lowest point in the United
States. From a geographical stand
point, the proposed investigation is
important for the additional reason
that Mount Whitney is considered the
highest peak within the boundaries of
the country.
According to mercurial tests, which
arc deemed by Government employes
unsatisfactory and liable to error,
Mcunt Whitney is anywhere from
1.".,503 to 15,530 feet in height. Farmer,
who is to take charge of a corps of
Government employes at Mojave and
who will direct the running of a line
of level to Owens Lake, across the
Mojave desert, hopes to use the ac
quired information as a basis for a
calculation mathematically and scien
tifically correct.
The same investigation will show
the greatest natural depression in the
earth's crust. The surface of Death
Valley, popularly supposed to be the
lowest in the country, has been esti
mate* at from one to five hundred feet
below the level of the sea.
It is proposed by another survey to
run a precise level from Merced into
the Yosemite Valley via Merced Falls
and Coultervllle, branching off at
Kingsley Postpfflce. and following the
path proposed by the Yosemite Val
ley commission as a practical railroad
route into the valley.
RAILROAD IS BUSY.
Lobbyists Working for Bill Once Ve
toed by Governor.
SACRAMENTO, Jan. 17.âJere Burke
and Walter Parker, political agents of
the Southern Pacific, are again busy in
Sacramento. Their time was well oc
cupied to-day ln the Senate and As
sembly lobbying on behalf of a bill pre
sented by Senator Pendleton, which the
existing railroads of California are anx
ious to have passed, the same measure
having been killed by the Governor's
veto two years ago. The bill aims to
amend section 1240 of tne Code of Civil
Procedure, the amendment being in
cluded in the following paragraph:
' All railroad crosslnge, when physically prac
ticable, shall be made either under or over
the existing railroad which it Is dtstred to
oro!"*, and If crossing In any case shall be
physically Impracticable, then the pronosM
level or grade crossing shall be protected by
the installation and maintenance of an auto
matic switch and signal service, with derailing
switches, to be Installed and thereafter to be
maintained at the expense of the owners of the
railroad desiring to make such crossing.
Jibw here is the point of the bill, as
mapped out by members of the Senate
that purpose vigorously opposing It:
The existing railroads of California
have a pretty thorougn understanding
among themselves as to policies of com
petition and rates, but no one can tell
.iust what the next road to get a foot
hold may do. So it Is just as well that
the future hold visions of the tremen
dous expense for the corporations yet
to come. And again the Southern Pa
cific and other roads were here first
and hence desire that they should not
be compelled to share the expense of
overhead and underground crossings in
the event such provision is made in any
future franchises to be granted.
It is pointed out that the proponents
of the measure can hardly ask for its
passage on the ground of safety, for it
malfi no provision for the correction
of the present flat crossings that check
the State from end to end.

STRANGE FIND
IN MAN'S EAR
Defect in Hearing Caused by
Sprouting Grain of Wheat
and a Good-Sized Pebble
OBSTRUCTIONS REMOVED
Keswick Business Man Pays
Visit to Bedding Physician
and Results are Startling
Special Dispatch to The Call.
REDDING, Jan. 17.âWhen Dr. C. W.
Bryan of this city examined the ear
of Joseph Bucher, a well-known busi
ness man of Keswisk, he whistled with
surprise, and with reason. He found
imbedded therein a grain of wheat and,
strange to relate, the wheat waa
sprouting.
Carefully removing the obstruction
the doctor gave the Bucher ear a
thorough cleaning and told the Kes
wick man that in the future his hear
ing would be perfect. Several days
later Bucher against visited the doc
tor and complained that his aural ap
pendage was still far from perfect. The
doctor made a second examination and
again he whistled, this time much
longer and louder than when he dis
covered the sprouting wheat.
The second search revealed a pebble
three-eighths of an inch long and one
eighth of an Inch wide. According to
Bucher's best recollections the pebble
had been in his ear about twelve years.
The grain of wheat, the doctor says,
must have been in the ear about a
twelvemonth.

3NEWS OF THE COUNTY OF ALAMEDA
COLD OF SHENG FOR STUDENTS
Powerful Chinese Prince
Establishes Scholarship
Professor Fryer Believes Others Will Follow
Berkeley Office San Francisco Call,
2148 Center street, Jan. 17.
His Excellency Sheng, mightiest of
all the great men in China, has been
induced by Professor John Fryer, of
the department of Oriental languages
at the State University, to provide five
scholarships for Chinese students now
at" "the university.
The first installment of the funds has
been received by Professor Fryer, and
four of the five fortunate Chinese
students have been selected as bene
ficiaries of their distinguished country
man's liberality. The students chosen
are later to be supplied with employ
ment by Sheng, in China, and already
can dream dreams and see visions of
political place and preferment at the
hands of the powerful Sheng.
This is the first instance on record,
according to Professor Fryer, who is
an authority on Oriental affairs and
thoroughly versed in all the history of
the Orient's relations with the West,
that scholarships have been given by
a Chinese official to the students of
any American or European university.
. The act of the Chinese official is the
result of an interview had with Pro
fessor Fryer last June when he called
upon Sheng at the latter's home in
Shanghai. These students are here on
their own resources and have difficul
ty in paying their own way.
Sheng is head of the railway and
telegraph administrations in China as
as well as in control of nearly all the
other important organizations for the
introduction of western learning and
methods into China. He is at the head
of the liberal party, his position being
greater and his power more extensive
than any other man in the kingdom,
RUSSIA'S SOIL
LOSING VALUE
Professor Hilgard Is to
Make Study of the Fa
mous "Black Earth" Lands
Â«
Berkeley Office San Francisco Call,
2148 Center street, Jan. 17.
Professor E. W. Hilgard, dean of the
college of agriculture of the University
of California, and the college have been
signally honored by the imperial Rus
sian Government by being requested to
give study to the causes of decay in
the soil of the famous "black earth"
region in European Russia, accounted
as one of the greatest wheat producing
districts in the world.
Professor Hilgard has received sam
ples of the earth with a request to con
duct a thorough scientific investigation
and to furnish a report for the guid
ance of the Czar's representatives in
their effort to revive the soil that is
rapidly becoming non-productive. Ana
lytical experiments and study will be
commenced at once by Dr. Lough
ridge, chief expert on soils in the col
lege, under Professor Hilgard's direc
tion, and report will be furnished to the
Russian Government at an early date.
The district, comprising 300,000 square
miles, washed by the rivers Don,
Dnieper and Dniester, is wearing out.
There is a gradual decay that the au
thorities fear will, unless checked, end
disastrously. Conditions have caused
an exodus of farmers and unless rem
edies are applied the Government sees
only depopulation of a once prosperous
and important farming community. It
is to learn what can be done to save
the district that appeal has been made
to the Berkeley scientists, whose world
wide fame has won them this important

PREPARE TO ELEVATE
THE STUDY OF HISTORY
Prominent Educators Meet and For
mulate Plans to Carry Out an
Important Work.
A LAMBDA, Jan. 17.âPlans have
been prepared by the committee ap
pointed by the Pacific Coast Branch
of the American Historical Society, of
which committee Dr. George S.
Thompson, principal of the Alameda
High School, is chairman, for a cam
paign throughout the West for the
promotion of the study of history. The
committee consisting of Dr. Thompson,
Professor Henry Morse Stephens of
the University of California, Professor
Cannon of Stanford University, Dr.
Rockwell D. Hunt, president of the*
San Jose High School, and Professor
E. T. Miller of the Chico High School,
met at the home of Dr. Thompson
here yesterday. It was decided that
the members of the committee would
visit the schools of the larger centers
of population and send circulars into
the smaller towns to ascertain the
course being pursued in the different
places in teaching history. The infor
mation thus derived will be embodied
in a report to the major organization.

AT WRONG TIME
Angry Intruder Attempts to

Scatters Belligerent Rela
tives of Client Who Seeks
Protection From His Wife
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
1016 Broadway, Jan. 17.
In an attempt to force Max Gold
man, his brother-in-law, to make terms
in a pending divorce suit, Harry
Barasch, flanked by his mother, Mrs.
Minnie Barasch, and his sister, Mrs.
Pauline J. Goldman, attacked Goldman
ln the offices of his attorney, D. F,
McWade, in the Blake & Moffltt
building, this afternoon, attempted to
strike McWade, and was only pre
vented from continuing by
McWade's action in forcibly ejecting
the husky intruder from his offices.
The trouble, which was caused by
Barasch's untoward conduct, was the
sequel to a long standing contest that
has been waged in the courts of two
counties between Goldman and his
wife. Some time ago Goldman, a tailor,
employed by A. S. Cohn & Co., 1071
Broadway, was sued at Martinez by
Mrs. Goldman for a divorce on the
ground of cruelty. Attorney M.
Ralph Jones took charge of her case.
Goldman resisted the charges and filed
through Attorney McWade a cross
complaint against his wife. After a
preliminary contest, McWade had the
suit transferred to Alameda County,
where it awaits further action.
Matters did not move to the satis
faction of Mrs. Goldman and her rela
tives. To-day the wife with her moth
er and her brother appeared at Gold
man's place of employment. They
flaunted legal documents at him and
demanded he should sign them. The
tailor, who is no match physically for
his brother-in-law, scented trouble. He
refused to deal with his callers and
referred them to Attorney McWade.
The trio descended upon the lawyer, to
whom also repaired Goldman.
After* considerable wrangling Mc-
Wade informed the three that he
should continue to do business with
their attorney. Then, left alone for a
moment, with Goldman he advised his
client to pay no attention to demands
for alimony, settlement or anything
else connected with the case unless he
should first consult his attorney. In
the midst of the talk Barasch and
Mrs. Goldman returned. Words passed
among the husband, his wife and her
brother when suddenly Barasch opened
battle by smashing at Goldman. Mc-
Wade intervened and was rewarded
with a drive from Barasch's fist which
missed connections.
To end the scene' McWade, assert
ing his right to peace in his own of
fice, displayed sufficient force to scat
ter his objectionable callers, who
rushed out of the apartments vowing
vengeance.
The Barasches made for the Polic
Court, but were not granted warrants
They declared they had been driven
out of the office by threats of shoot
Ing. â¢

MOTHER TELLS
OF FATAL CALL
Describes Incidents Leading
to the Murder of Daughter
by Lemuel D. Campbell
BEARS HIM NO ILL WILL
Testifies to Admitting Her
Son-in-Law Into the House
and of Hearing Interview
Oakand Office San Francisco Call,
1016 Broadway, Jan. 17.
Hannah Cutler, the aged mother of
Laura J. W. Campbell, who was shot
jto death by her husband, Lemuel D.
Campbell, at the paternal home at 1362
! Eleventh street, in November last, was
1 the first witness called by the prosecu-
I tion this afternoon. In a dramatic
I part of her testimony under cross-ex
! amination she swore with uplifted hand
j that she had no hard feeling against
, her former son-in-law and exclaimed,
I"I would not hurt him if I could."
Mrs. Cutler is bent with years and
wrinkled, but her mind is bright, and
she remembers clearly the incidents re
lating to the fatal call. She testified
that she let Campbell into the house,
and to his salutation, "Good evening,
mother," she answered "Good evening,
| Lem; come in." She saw her daughter
accompany him to the door and heard
her say, "Now, be a good boy and go
away and come some other time."
Then she heard the shot and her
daughter's scream.
Campbell, she says, had been at their
house about an hour and a half. He
was ugly from drinking and wanted
his wife to come back to him. They
talked together for a long time.
It was not until after noon that the
jury was completed. The jurors are:
Jacob B. Tallmah. Frederick G. Welp,
William Langstaff, Henry Menges, E.
L. Sargent, Theodore Swift, John Bar
net, David McCarrom. Benjamin
Wright, John Soule, J. R. Taylor and
William Carrish.
FRESHMEN HAVE
HONOR SOCIETY
OF THEIR OWN
BERKELEY. Jan. IT.âA new honor society
ha* been organized at the university, the fresh
man class having decided to emulate the upper
classmen, who long ago organized such so
cieties as the Golden Bar and Winged Helmet.
The new honor society was founded by fresh
men who are especially Interested ln Inor
ganic chemistry. A constitution has been
drawn up and officers have been elected as
follows: President, A. C. Meyer; vice presl
dent, A. C. North; secretary and treasurer,
E. O. Slater. Dr. Morgan and Professoi
Booth have been elected honorary members.
Professor Booth's aid having been extended In
the formation of the society. The organization
will be known as the "Inorganic Chemistry
Society." High scholarship in chemistry will
be the requisite fo membership.
The university has received a collection of
800 art pieces brought from the republic of
Colombia as the gift of Cornelius Rumsey
of Riverside. The collection has been In
stalled In the anthropological museum In the
Affiliated â¢ Colleges in San Francisco.
The number of "cinches" this year Is forty
less than last, only 204 students having been
"cinched" by Recorder Sutton, as against 244
last term. Several prominent men are said to
have fallen by the wayside In their examina
tions, among these, according to report, being
Claude Kern, last year on the varsity football
team, and R. Bliss, custodian of the Stanford
ax, and a prominent baseball player.
The registration figures thus far made up by
Recorder Sutton Indicate an Increase ln regis
tration over last term. There were nearly one
hundred more students enrolled on the first
day than at the same time in 1004. the total
being 2020 as against 1033 last year.
The try-outs for the Carnot debating team
were held to-night in Stiles Hall. Thirteen
men entered. The results will be announced
to-morrow. The Carnot debate will be held
with Stanford late in the spring. Seven of
the ten medals that have been awarded ln
these contests have come to California. Stan
ford this year has a slight advantage, as
Sheriffs, who was on last year's team at Stan
ford, Is there now. None of the thirteen men
who were tried out to-night have ever taken
part in an intercollegiate contest. The list
Includes E. C. Nathan, E. C. Baker, G. C.
Rmgolsky, G. B. Glllson. W. J. Musgrove. W.
E. Moroney, H. L. Hagan, W. 8. Robinson,
C It Smith, J. S. Joford, E. M. Pelxotto. N.
A. Eisner, M. E. Harrison. The last named
Is the only freshman among the contestants.
Professors Gayley, Flaherty, Richardson,
Overstreet. Mitchell and Yon Neumayer acted
as judges to choose three men for the team
and one for alternate.
The work'of reconstructing the south drive
on the campus has been In progress during the
Christmas vacation and Is now well under
way. It is to cost 110,000. The road extends
from the Dana street entrance ln front of
Stiles Hall to the chemistry building. The
roadbed Is to be macadamized. The gutters
adjoining are of stone and cement. The curb
Is of 6-lnch concrete and continuous with the
gutter. A cement sidewalk five feet broad
Is to be built next to the curb on the south
side of the road. This form of road will be
a model for all to be constructed
In the future on the campus.
JACOB RIIS LEAVES TOWN
FOR SOUTHERN CALIFORNIA
Well Known Philanthropist Thanks
the People Who Made His Stay
Here Pleasant.
Jacob Rils, the well known lecturer
and philanthropist, left yesterday for
Southern, California. Before leaving
he gave out the following letter of ap
preciation:
To the Editor of The Callâl cannot leave
San Francisco without saying a word of
thanks, along with a hearty goodby, to its
people who hive welcomed me as. It seems to
rte, stranger was never welcomed before. To
the California Club, to the men and women of
the Commonwealth Club, and to the scores
Whose kindly courtesy will live long ln my
memory and makes it just a bit hard to go
away, my heartfelt thanks. No one knows
better than I how small Is the deserving that
has met with such a welcome, but on behalf
of the men and women m the East who are
lighting for better things for their poorer
brethren and whom in some measure here I
represent. I accept it all gladly and gratefully
as a token that we are brothers, one ln aim
and purpose and ln honest effort, and as an
earnest of the better day that is coming to
our cities and to our land as surely as we
keep up the fight and never let go. I have
seen enough here to know that the same shad
ows, the same jjroblems are dogging your steps
that have overtaken us In our swift growth
into metropolitan greatness, and I have found,
too, the same unconquerable forces for good
that are winning our fight and will win yours,
that in the end must eternally prevail as sure
as good is stronger than evil ln God's world.
All good luck to you and godspeed.
Gratefully yours,
JACOB A. RIIS

READY TO TEST
STATE'S WHEAT
Professor Shaw Will Soon Begin
Important Experiments.
Machinery for Work Arrives From the East
BERKELEY, Jan. 17.âProfessor
G. W. Shaw of the agricultural de
partment of the State University re
turned to-day from Portland, Or., to
begin the task of analyzing wheat
grown ln California, to the end that
the amount of gluten it contains may ,
be shown and thereby the product of
the State be put in a class which as
yet it has not reached.
The machinery for ihese important
analyses has just arrived from, the
East, with the exception of a few
pieces of apparatus that are to come
from San Francisco. Professor Shaw
to-day attended to the task of put
ting this machinery in place, with the
assistance of skilled mechanics.
Bags of wheat now litter the floor
of the room occupied by Professor
Shaw, each labeled to show in which
part of California the grain was
grown. While in Oregon Professor
Shaw tested the wheat of that State,
making thorough examination of all
Its properties. The results will be me
bodied in bulletins to be issued later.
The wheat of the north, 'however, is
not superior to the California product,
according to Shaw.
The agricultural department of the
university has this work of experi
menting and analysis of wheat ln
charge, . with Professor Wickson su
perintending the investigation, Pro
fessor Shaw performing the practical
work of analysis. Thousands of dol
lars have been raised by the State
Board of Trade and other commercial
bodies to prosecute the work and the j
State Legislature is to be asked to ap- I
propriate $10,000 to carry it forward.

following:

more valuable It is as a food. California
wheat Is said to be deficient in that property.

MORE BOUNTY FRAUDS
UNEARTHED IN UTAH
Scalps Sold Twice Through the Con
nivance of the County Officers
In the State.
OGDEN, Utah, Jan. 17. âFollowing
the revelations ln Salt Lake County
of scalp bounty frauds aggregating
nearly $17,000 came the announce
ment to-day that Weber County has
been defrauded of $14,000 within a
few months. The arrest of William
R. Swan, deputy county clerk, and
Arthur H. Conslik, son of a former
Chief of Police, was effected to-day.
They are charged with perjury and
conspiracy and obtaining money by
false pretenses.
As in Salt Lake, duplicate bounty
was collected by plugging the holes
punched in the scalps by the County
Clerk first paying the bounty.

the State and see which locality has the best
grain in the matter of gluten. The experiment
is expected to prove of great value to tne
wheat growing Industry and the State at large.
The process of determining how much gluten
there Is In wheat Is not very complicated. I
shall first apply what is called the gluten
ttst. I shall separate the gluten from the â 
starch ln the wheat and then analyse the
.gluten itself. To get the gluten separate the .
dough made from the wheat will be held j
under a stream of water until all the starch
grains have become separated from the "&â¢ u *
mass of gluten and are washed away. WM ,
the wash water no longer contains any sedi
ment of starch I shall know the gluten Is
not alloyed. The wet gluten will be weighed
and a second weighing will be done when it
has dridd so that comparisons may be made.
Thus we 'will find the power of the gluten to
hold water, an important point. .Then I shall
stretch the gluten Into threads, to determine
its strength and tenacity. The longer and
finer the threads the better the gluten. Gluten
is the quality ln wheat that makes possible
the expansion of the loaf when the dough is
put into the over. The raising power of the
loaf Is dependent on the expansion of the
gluten.
The gluten will then be put into a standard
cylinder, which has a tight-fitting piston rod,
and will be compressed under even pressure.
Then the gluten, with all the water squeezed
from it, will go into an electric oven and we .
will note the expansion that results as Indl- I
eating the quality. First-class gluten should |
contain 65 per cent of glladln and 35 per cent (
of glutenln. the former indicating its ex- 1
panaive power and the latter Its cohesive
qualities.
The color test, so called, Is used on sam
ples of flour In this way: Different grades of
flour are spread on plate glass and smoothed
with -a steel spatula. Colored glass slabs are
used of varying color from light to gray to
brown. The flours are matched with glasses
and the color of the glass Is recorded which
corresponds with the color of the flour.
An interesting feature of the ex
periments Just begun by Professor
Shaw, following the analyses he will
make, is the cross-polinating of
wheats that are found to be superior
in glutenous qualities. The wheats
found to be strongest and finest will
be crossed to produce such effects as
plant and animal breeders accomplish
in their crossing of breeds.
PRESENCE OF JAPANESE
A BAR TO INSURANCE
Companies Think Growing Hostility
to Brown Labor Entails Too
Great a Risk.
SEATTLE, January 17.âWithin the
past few days several local fire insur
ance agents have received instructions
not to write policies upon sawmills or
shingle mills with which Japanese
are in any way identified, either , as
employes ln any capacity or as
owners. Â«
Underwriters dd not precisely agree
as to the reason. It is agreed, how
ever, that the most important factor
is a growipg hostility to Japanese
labor on the part of white laborers,
which is thought to be liable to result
at any time in 1 physical violence, with
consequent danger to property
through open trouble or incendiarism.

Holmes on Russian Empire.
A large audience as usual greeted
Burton Holmes at Lyric Hall last
evening upon the occasion of his
fourth illustrated "Travelogue " "The
Russian Empire." After an exhibition
of the great Russian cities and a de
scription of their social and political
life from the peasant's hut to the pal
ace of the noble, the audience was taken
with the lecturer completely across
Asia on the Trans-Siberian railway to
Manchuria, viewing through the me
dium of the biograph many places and
peoples. Mr. Holmes' detailed knowl
edge of his subject, together with its
timeliness because of the present war,
made it of' exceptional interest.

CARNEGIE WILL
STAND LOSSES
â¢
Agrees to Reimburse Oberlin
students Who Suffered
Through Chadwick Affair
Â«
OBERLIN, Ohio, Jan. 17.âGratifying
j news was received by the students of
Oberlin College to-day, when President
King announced in chapel that all
moneys which had been lost by stu
dents through the failure of the Citi
zens' National Bank, which closed its
doors at the time of the alleged Chad
: wick forgeries, would be paid on pre
i sentation of passbooks.
President King announced, further,
that Andrew Carnegie was the man
who teas doing this. Carnegie, Presi
dent King stated, while innocently fig
uring in the bank failure, and in no
wise responsible, did not wish to see
deserving students and others suffer.
Besides reimbursing the depositors,
Carnegie reimburses the Young Men's
Christian Association to the extent of
some $3000, which had been laid aside
for a new building, and which was lost
in the same failure.

DEGRADED IN PUBLIC
| Sailor of the Askold to Serve Term
in French Prison in
Shanghai.
SHANGHAI, Jan. 17.âThe sailor
belonging to the Russian cruiser Ask
old, who on December 15 murdered a

Fire Burns for Two Days.
CHICAGO, Jan. 18.âAfter forty
eight hours of continuous fighting the
fire in the storage warehouse of
Schwarzschild & Sulzberger at the
Union Stockyards was placed under
control shortly before midnight. The
contents of the warehouse are entirely
lost, but the walls of the building are
unharmed. The loss is estimated at
$600,000.
