TURNS AGAINST
THE EPPINGERS
Warehouse Superintendent
Demings Says Firm Mixed
All Its Grades of Wheat
EVIDENCE IS IMPORTANT
May Mean Pro Rata Appor
tionment of the Entire
Sum of Money at Stake
After several days of investigation i
j as to the handling of grain in the ware- |
â â  house of Eppinger & Co., at Crockett, ;
James Demings, the superintendent,
i too% the stand yesterday and testified
j that it would be almost an impossibil
! lty to separate the pile of grain,
i amounting to* 3500 tons, at present in
J the warehouse, into lots giving each of
j the creditors his full share. The banks
jof this city and throughout the State
\ hold warehouse receipts for lots of
i grain, but the testimony of Demings
| conclusively proved hat the
! paid no attention to the numbers on
; each lot, but mingled them and pre-
I sented the result as the numbers asKed
â i * or *
', Demings testified that when a ship
i came for grain he would telephone to j
j the Eppinger office in San Francisco,
j and received orders to deliver the
; wheat lying nearest to the berth of the
j vessel, irrespective of the lot number.
[In many cases this was done, and
; when, for instance, lot No. 720 was
! called for a portion of lot No. 706 would
jbe given, and the numbers would be
j changed, so as to give the impression
i that the right grain was being given
j out. This practice went on for some ,
| time until, in the witness' opinion, it |
j was impossible to Identify the sepa- j
; rate lots.
A memorandum book used by Dem-
I ings was the cause of much argument ;
! between Frank H. Powers and W. H. j
j Chickerlng as to its admission. The '
] book contained what purported to be j
a segregated list of each lot of grain, \
I and therefore supposed to be of use in j
i identification. It was shown, however, I
| that the book was compiled from the
| missing books of the firm, and it could
â¢ not be admitted. It was also brought
I out that a great deal of the wheat was
I mixed with barley, and that the iden- i
j tification of individual lots was impos- ;
: sible.
Jacob Eppinger was called to the
stand during the morning session, but
under instructions of his counsel, j
James H. Budd, he refused to answer
any question on the ground that it )
might tend to incriminate him.
GRAND JURY IS ACTIVE.
Again Indicts the Eppingers and Ac
â¢ cuscs Election Commissioners.
The Grand Jury returned a new ac
cusation against the members of the
Board of Election Commissioners yes
terday, praying that they be removed
from office. The charge is similar to
that filed by the former inquisitorial
body, only correcting the technical de
fect that caused Judge Lawlor to dis
miss the original accusation.
Messrs. Robert W. Roberts, James
A. Devoto, E. C. Leffingwell, Thomas
Maguire and A. W. Voorsanger. who
constitute the board, will be served
with notice to-day and given ten days
in which to answer. There was no es
caping of prosecution, for some time
ago the Merchants' Association an
nounced that it would prefer charges
against the Commissioners if the ma
chinery of the law usually set in
operation in such cases did not move.
The allegations of misfeasance are
not new, being those set forth in the
Lilienfeld jury's accusation and in
the charges under which the Mayor
is at present investigating the conduct
of the board. It Is charged that of
ficers for the Seventy-third and Eigh
tieth precincts were appointed for the
primary election held last AuguÃt who
were neither on the assessment roll
nor residents of those Drecincts, and
that three of them were at the time
holding salaried positions under the
municipal government. It was in
these two precincts that the frauds
were committed.
The Grand Jury also presented ad
ditional indictments yesterday against
Jacob Eppinger, Hermann Eppinger,
Josua Eppinger and James Demings
in connection with the grain frauds.
The accused were indicted several
days ago, but yesterday' s charges
were on new counts. The Grand Jury
is bent on having successful prosecu
tions made in the gigantic swindle by
means of bogus warehouse certificates.
Bernard Ettlinger, who was a member
of the firm of Eppinger & Co., is
said to have furnished the jurors with
much new incriminating evidence.
Demings was superintendent of the
warehouses at Crockett that were de
void of wheat, though banks were
lending the firm hundreds of thou
sands of dollars on certificates repre
sented to be issued there.
James Demings was arrested yester
day afternoon and released on $15,000
bonds furnished by his father and
Captain Erickson. Jacob and Her
mann Eppinger were arrested last night
and each released on $15,000 bonds fur
nished by a surety company.
At a late hour last night Josua
Eppinger had not been found.
WILL VISIT ALL THEATERS.
Works Commissioners Declare That
No Favoritism Will Be Shown.
The Grand Jury committee, accom
panied by City Architect Shea and
Chief Engineer Shaughnessy, will In
spect all the theaters to-day. The
Board of Works has declared that no
discrimination will be shown and that
any theater which does not comply
with the regulations will be closed.
Upon application of Attorney Henry
C. McPike for the Baldwin Theater,
and Julius Kahn for the Unique The
ater, Judge Seawell yesterday granted
a temporary order restraining Chief
Wittman and the Board of Public
Works from closing those places of
amusement under directions of the
Grand Jury. An order .to show cause
why the injunction should not be
made permanent was made returnable
next Friday morning. The Baldwin
management recites that the place has
just been remodeled to lit require
ments of the Board of Works and
that the closing would entail great
less. _

How everything comes out right in

I may be seen at the Alcazar. One meets

J types, the character of a landlady,

i sequence considers herself privileged
I to impose worse ones upon the board

humorous, womanly and mannerly In
; the part. Miss Christie McLean as

BLANCHE PARTINGTON.
Â«
The Tivoli.
The audience that packed the Tiv
oli to its utmost limits went wild with
j enthusiasm* after the mad scene in
{ "Lucia" last evening and all on ac-
I count of the very remarkable vocal
; work of Signorina Tetrazzini. The dra-
I matic part of the scene did not count
i materially. In fart, it hardly figured
las an appreciable fact. But the sing
ing of Tetrazzini brought forth such
a demonstration of approval as will
be memorable in the annals of the
Tlvoli whenever they shall be writ
ten. It began with "bravos," deep
ened into American cheers and was
prolonged until the artist had been
before the curtain half a score of
times. The din sounded from the or
chestra chairs to the top gallery,
j which was densely packed with an
i audience of Italian temperament. No
I greater compliment has ever been
j paid to a singer in San Francisco.
Never was a demonstration more de
served. All this was in despite of the
fact tha/t Tetrazzin? had a cold. Her
art and purity of tone carried the day
completely, notwithstanding this
I drawback. It was a Tetrazzini night,
emphatically.
Slgnor Colli was the Edgardo. In
the last scene he secured "bravos"
and was at his best. La Puma was
the Enrico, De Marco the Arthur.
Oddly enough the sextet was given
only once and then wlth # flve instead
of six principal singers. The opera
was conducted Golischiani.
The chorus work was good. A fea
ture of the performance was the fine
harp playing of Signora Carusl.

Titles of Naval Staff Officers.
Civil Engineer Peter C. Asserson, U-.
S. N., was retired from active service
with the rank of rear admiral and the
corresponding pay, but when he used
the title of "rear admiral" upon his
visiting card a line officer of similar
grade protested to the Navy Depart
ment that Mr. Asserson, being a staff
officer, was unauthorized to use a title
that "belonged only to officers of the
navy empowered to actually command
ships and squadrons." The Secretary
of the Navy, by directing Mr. Asserson
not to use the title of rear admiral,
either in official or personal corre
spondence, practically supports the
contention of the line officer.
It is said that an effort is to be made
ln the next Congress to correct this an
omaly by authorizing the staff officer
to assume the corresponding line title.
Could not the' problem be solved by
some appropriate affix to the more fa
miliar and more generally honored title
âas engineer rear admiral, surgeon
rear admiral, pay rear admiral? There
is already a precedept for such action
in the case of the army, where we now
have surgeon general, paymaster gen
eral and quartermaster general. Some
such law authorizing the use on all oc
casions of a distinctive and sufficiently
honorable title would remove much
friction.â Engineering News.
Curse of the Permanent Job.
It is interestingâand possibly im
portantâto note that the chief cause
of matrimonial failures is the chief
cause of failure in other directions.
It is what may be called the curse of
the "permanent job." Give a man what
is, or seems to him to be, a permanent
Job, and he begins to go to seed. Am
bition dies, industry withers, skill dries
up. Before it is too late, transfer him
to a Job where there are but two cer
taintiesâthat he can keep it if he does
well, that he will lose it if he doesn't.
At once there is an amazing change;
and the man who was degenerating
into a sour, querulous failure is devel
oping into a cheerful, useful success.
The husband who says: "Now I've got
her," and ceases <o try to pleaseâor
the wife who acts irf the same spiritâis
on the way to matrimonial disaster.
Whether or not the marriage was made
in heaven, it's got to be lived on earth.
Nor are the laws of human nature sus
pended for the benefit of the married.â
Saturday Evening Post.

Paris as a Diplomatic Center.
The tribunal which is dealing with
the controversy relating to. Russia's
sinking of English fishing craft in the
North Sea has Just opened at the
French capital. Paris is resuming its
old glory as the world's polttical cen
ter. It was at Paris that the repre
sentatives of the United States and
Spain met to settle the issues growing
out of the war of 1898. It seemed Just
as appropriate a place for the United
States and Spain to meet in that Junc
ture as it did ln 1856* for England.
Russia, Prussia, Austria. Turkey and
the rest of the European powers to deal
with the issues growipg out of the
Crimean war. It was at a congress at
Paris in 1783 that England recognised
United States independence. Twenty
years earlier a treaty of Paris brought
the seven years' war to an end. Be
tween 17(53 and 1904 more important in
ternational gatherings have been held
at Paris than ln all the rest of the
world's capitals.âGlobe Democrat.

CORINTHIAN TEAM DEFEATED
BY LICK AT BASKET BALL
Large Crowd Witnesses the Game in
Gymnasium of the Young Men's
Hebrew Association.
The Lick team defeated the Corin
thians at basket ball last night in the
gymnasium of the Young Men's He
brew Association, 1970 Page street. A
large number of enthusiasts were
present and cheered for their favor
ite team. The flnal score was 11 to 5.
The Lick boys won by having more
experience and displaying superior
knowledge of the game. Several
times during the play the members of
the Corinthians fouled, thereby giv
ing their opponents a chance to score
try goals. Doane of the Licks played
well. He scored three try goals and
one field goal for his team. Bromley,
his team mate, succeeded in placing
the ball in the basket twice from dif
ferent positions.
The most sensational work of the
game was done by Kamp of the Corin
thians. He scored field goals from a
long distance and won applause from
the spectators. A delegation of root
ers from the Lick School occupied one
side of the gymnasium and created
considerable noise with their school
yell.
The teams lined up as follows:
LickâDe Wolf, Bromley, Rodgers,
Stone, Strauss and Bowie. Corin
thians â Kamp, Welfleld, Lipman,
Spiro, Josef. Schoor and Jacobs,
EGAN'S SURETIES FILE
AFFIDAVITS OF HOLDINGS
Commissioner of Works Satisfies Audi,
tor Baehr That Official Bond
Is Sufficient.
Commissioner of Public Works
Egan, whose official bond was not ac
cepted by Auditor Baehr. yesterday
filed affidavits showing the kind of
property owned by his two sureties,
Edward McGary and M. A. Murphy.
McGary's possessions consist of 120
acres of Improved land near Pacheco,
Cal., valued at $8500, but mortgaged
for $3600; one-sixth of the capital
stock in the McGary estate, consisting
for the most part of real estate hold
ings, his share being valued at $25,
--000, and one-seventh of the capital,
stock of the Pacific States Refineries,
a corporation, valued at $6000.
Murphy's affidavit avers that he is
possessed of fifty-seven acres of
orange lands near East Riverside val
ued at $57,000. and sixty-five acres of
fruit land in San Bernardino County.
Baehr's attorney advised him that the
property is sufficient to cover the
bonds, which will be accepted as soon
as the Mayor, who was out of town
yesterday, arrives at his office.
Treats of Burbank's Work.
An article in the California Promo
tion Committee's magazine, For Cali
fornia, is devoted to the work of
Luther Burbank, the plant wizard.
Other features are "Statements of New
Settlers' First Year in California,"
"What One Settler Did," by Colvin B.
Brown, secretary of the Stockton
Chamber of Commerce; "Second Meet
ing of the Counties Committee,"
"What Settlers Say," "Review of Irri
gation and Development Work in
1904," "One For All and All For One,"
"The Brown Family's First Tear in
California," "Imports of Poultry, But
ter, Eggs and Meat Products in 1904."
Illustrations are by Anna Hem.

BLAMES RACES
FOR HIS FALL
Elmer Smith, Who Stole to
Buy Finery for the Ogden
Girls, Faces Prison Term
Special Dispatch to The Call.
ST. LOUIS. Jan. 17.âElmer Smith's
invalid wife, who returned home only
a few weeks ago from the hospital,
suffered a relapse to-day upon learn
ing from the newspapers of her hus
band's thefts from the postofflce and
his attentions to Rita and Pauline Og
den of San Francisco. Her physician
to-night reported her condition as crit
ical.
Smith's bonds were fixed to-day at
$2600, which, he says, his family will
provide. The bond on the charge of
defrauding the Government is fixed at
$1000; the one for conspiracy is $1600.
His trial on the charge of defrauding
the Government is set for the May
term of the United States District
Court. His preliminary hearing on the
charge of conspiracy will take place on
Friday before a United States commis
sioner.
The Ogden girls, who have been re
leased, to-day left their flat by order
of the police. A sign "For rent" now
appears on the front door.
Smith does not appear to realize the
seriouÃ nature of his offense, and faces
the future as untroubled as a man go
ing to success. Instead of a probable
term in prison.
"It all began with the races," Smith
said to-day. "Last summer I went to
the race tracks, and the first thing I
did was to make a couple of hundred.
It came so easy I tried it again. I was
lucky for a while. I made a killing
of $1600 one day, and that night went
out to spend it. That was when I met
Rita. I lost as much as $1000 at a
clip. To make good again and get
another roll, I stole $1000 from the ex
press company. I had given Rita a
good deal of money, and when the ex
press shortage turned up she gave me
back $800, and I got $200 out of the
postofflce money orders and made It
good.
"I want to say right here that the
Ogden girls did not know how I was
getting the money. I told them I was
a plunger on the races and made my
money that way and had a good bank
account
"At first I believed I could make it
all back in a bunch and get out-even.
Then I met Rita, and didn't care, so
long as I could be with her."
Boxers Released From Jail.
LEAVENWORTH, Kan., Jan. 17.â
Mexican Pete Everett and Thomas
Tracy, pugilists, known throughout
the country, were released from the
Federal prison to-day after serving a
year for engaging in a prize fight at
Punca, Oklahoma, in violation of the
Federal law.
HENRY D. JACKS COMMITS SUICIDE.â
Henry D. Jacks. 50 years old. of 120 Eighth
street, formerly employed as a solicitor of
the California Products Company ln the
Crocker building-, committed suicide last night
by taking cyanide of potassium. He was his
wife's second husband and had frequenUy
auarreled with her.
TRIES SUICIDE;
BLAMES LAWSON
Man Who Lost Fortune in
Wall Street Attempts to
Jump Off Brooklyn Bridge
NEW YORK, Jan. to
have lost his fortune of 180,000 as a
result of Thomas W. Lawson's attacks
upon Wall street Interests, Frank D.
Austin, 40 years old, twice to-day at
tempted to throw himÃelf from the
Brooklyn bridge. He was prevented
by Captain Devanny of the bridge
police, who grappled with him, and.
after a long struggle in which he was
helped by two detectives, placed him
under arrest.
Captain Devanny was warned that
the man was to make an attempt upon
his life by a pawnbroker whose place
of business is near the New York end
of the bridge. He told the captain that
the man had Just left his shop after
pawning some Jewelry, with the re
mark: "I guess the only thing left
for me is to Jump off the bridge."
With the two detectives the captain
found the man and rode closely behind
as he rode out on a trolley car on the
way across the bridge. The car had
Just passed the New York tower when
Austin sprang into the roadway that
separated him from the bridge railing.
The officers sprang after him and he
was subdued after a hard struggle.
He was put on another car, and sat
so quietly that the officers believed he
would make no more trouble. Sudden
ly, however, he Jumped from his seat
and was half way across the roadway
before the officers overtook him. Then
followed a most violent struggle, ln
which the three policemen were hardly
a match for him. Several women ln
the car were on the verge of panic
when Austin finally was subdued and
led back to the car.
"I wanted to end It all." he told his
captors on his way to the police sta
tion. "I have lost $80,000 in Wall
street, all on account of that frenzied
finance writer, Thomas W. Lawson."
To Keep Order at Pavilion.
The Board of Directors of the Me
chanics' Institute held Its semi-month
ly meeting last night at 81 Post street
and took occasion to reply to the let
ter of the Police Commission regarding
disorder and immorality at the Me
chanics' Pavilion during some of the
recent balls. The board ordered a
letter sent to the Commissioners say
ing that the lease for the Pavilion
provides that nothing against the law
shall be permitted by the lessees, and
requested the assistance of the police
to enforce the clause. The annual
election of seven directors will take
place on February 28.
Anglers' Club Is Organized.
A meeting was held last night at
which the Anglers' Club of California
was organized. J. H. Sammi was
elected temporary chairman and E. J.
Kaplan secretary and treasurer. The
first regular meeting will be held next
Monday night in Grant Hall, Pioneer
Building, when permanent officers will
be elected. Invitations have been sent
to all the prominent fishermen asking
them to attend.

COLLEGE OARSMEN TRAINING.
Bright Prospects for a Whining Crew
at Stanford University.
STANFORD UNIVERSITY, Jan. 17.
The boating season opened at Stan
ford this afternoon in the gymnasium,
where the candidates for the crew
were worked for an hour on the row
ing machines. Practice of this kind
will be gone through dally until Lagu
nltas Lake fills with water and regular
work In the barges can begin. A
dainty four-oared shell has been pur
chased in the East and the Stanford
crew will row this year's intercollegiate
boat race in the regulation weight boat
instead of the heavy barge used last
season. The Stanford crew was handi
capped hitherto because there was no
straightaway course on Lagunltas
Lake. When the race was run on the
rough water of the bay at Berkeley
the cardinal crew was so unused to
the course that it was never a con
tender for first place.
This year the Stanford crew will
have the advantage of practice on San
Francisco Bay. The City Council of
Redwood City has granted to Stanford
the privilege of erecting a temporary
boathouse on the city pier, which is ad
jacent to a course of deep water.
Of last year's varsity crew W. H.
Dole (captain), H. G. Butterneld. N. C.
Bryan and R. H. Gaither are back in
college and are again eligible. There
are also a large number of new men
who have had training and who will
try for positions in the shell. Profes
sor W. F. Durand, who was vitally in
terested in boating while at Cornell
University, will coach the men this sea
son.
BERKELEY, Jan. 17.âThe Univer
sity of California Boating Association
has been notified to remove its club
house on the Session basin, East Oak
land, by the new owners, the Oakland
Harbor Development Company, which
has purchased the lands from James
de Fremery. The association must sur
render possession within sixty days.
This order, also affects other boating
clubs on the basin. The property is to
be reclaimed for manufacturing pur
poses.
TROPHIES FOR TENNIS PLAYERS.
California Club Directors Offer Six
Cups for Competition.
The regular monthly meeting of the
California Tennis Club directors was
held last night at Scott and Bush
streets. The principal topic of discus
sion was the holding of tournaments
during the coming year. It was de
cided that six cups be offered by the
directors to be won three times. Two
of the trophies will be for handicap
singles tournaments and four for
class singles events. In the latter each
class will play on a different Sunday.
Dr. Hill and Dr. Lovegrove were ap
pointed to select the trophies.
Grant Smith was appointed a com
mittee of one to assist the tourna
ment committee in classifying the
players and arranging the handicaps.
The directors present were W. S. Mac-
Gavin, Dr. Hill, C. G. Kuehn, Dr.
Lovegrove, Dr. Noble and Grant
Smith. Absent, P. S. Mitchell.

O. A. Bremer, L. C Babln, A. Streck

ed the following officers: President,
Philo Jacoby; vice president L. J.
Reubold; secretaary, O. A. Bremer;
treasurer, Fred Levers.

ANDREW CLUNIE QUESTIONS
EXECUTOR E. A. BRIDGFORD
Statute Quoted as Excuse for Employ
ing Additional Attorneys in
Estate Proceedings.
E. A. Bridgford, one of the execu
tors of the estate of the late Thomas
J. Clunle, who, with Burrell G. White,
has been accused by Andrew J. Clu
nle with the misad ministration
and misapplication of funds, was
subjected to a two hours' examin
ation in Judge Coffey's court yester
day in a further hearing of Clunie's
objections to their account.
Judge Bridgford said that he had
been a Superior Judge at Woodlands
for fourteen years and for seven years
had practiced law in this city. He had
been Thomas J. Clunie's law partner,
but the partnership had been severed
before the latter's death.
Questioned upon his allowance of
$10,000 attorneys' fees to Hudson
Grant and Walter Robinson, Bridg
ford said that he relied on the provi
sions of the statute permitting execu
tors to pay reasonable attorneys' fees
as his authority for making the pay
ment. He considered the services ren
dered worth the amount paid and said
that the necessity for further legal
service was occasioned by Clunie's
question of the validity of the trust
clause of the will and by Mrs. Clu
nie's contest On the point of another
of Clunie's objections he said that
Mrs. Clunle had been paid $104,000
cash and the remainder to make
$160,000 ln securities, and that Clunie
had advanced cash to guarantee the
securities. This was the reason it was
entered as a cash payment. The hear
ing will be resumed this morning.
YOUNG MONNIER'S HABITS
SUBJECT OF ARGUMENTS
Attorney Roche Concludes and Collins
Begins Presentation of the Case
to Jury.
Theodore J. Roche of counsel for
George Monnler Jr. concluded his
presentation of the case to the Jury
in Judge Graham's court yesterday,
and in the afternoon George D. Col
lins spoke for two hours in behalf of
the widow, Anna Monnier, who Is de
fending the winemaker's will. Collins
will resume this morning.
Young Monnier's habits and tenden
cies threaded both arguments. Roche
contended that the boy was not re
sponsible for his gambling and drink
ing proclivities, having been taught a
taste for liquor by his quondam
parents when lie was four years of
age, and having been Initiated by
them into the mysteries of poker when
yet a boy. Roche declared that Col
lins, as attorney, knew that Monnier's
will, when it was drawn, absolutely
precluded the boy from ever sharing
in the estate. He dwelt, too, upon the
elder Monnier's uncertainty before
making the will, as Indicated in the tes
timony of the friends whom he had
consulted.
Collins declared that young Monnier
was, a degenerate, and if the Jury
brcke the will it would give him a
fortune to spend and send him on his
downward course to ruin. The attor
ney had much to say about alleged
false theories advanced by the other
side, and said that the case was so
made because the fortune "looked
good" to the attorneys. He said the
charge of unsoundness of mind had
fallen and that of the widow's undue
Influence had not been proved.

STANFORD GIRLS
IN BASKET BALL
Eighteen Students Go Into
Active Training for a
Series of Match Games
_ â¦ ââ
WILL MEET MJJH TEAMS
Contests Will Be Held iv
Nevada and in Some of
the Cities Near College
STANFORD UNIVERSITY, Jan. 17.
Eighteen Stanford girls have gone in
to active training for basketball and
for the next two months they will
practice faithfully every day under the
instruction of their trainer, J. C. Ca
hoon, '06. A complete schedule of
games ending with a series of three
games with the girls of the University
of California, has been arranged by the
manager, Miss Jane Spaulding. The
winner of this series of contests will
probably be the champion ladies' bas
ketball team of the coast.
The Stanford team la a member of
the Coast League and will play cham
pionship games with Nevada, Mllis
College, California and San Jose Nor
mal. Other contests will be held with
Palo Alto High School, Redwood, San
Mateo and with teams from San Fran
cisco.
Stanford expects to win all these
games as there are a number of ex
perienced players now ln college.
WOODCRAFT RECEIVES
THE GRAND GUARDIAN
C, a Van Orsdeii, Head of the Ordea,
Visits This City and Is Tendered
a Reception.
There was a grand rally last night
ln Native Sons' Hall of the local circles
of Woodcraft in honor of the visit to
this city of Grand Guardian Neighbor
Carrie C. Van Orsdeii, of Pendleton,
Ore.
The distinguished visitor /came to
San Francisco on Monday, and ln the
evening she was the guest of honor
at a banquet given by Organizer Miss
Bertha Sumner to the San Francisco
members of the campaign committee,
of which Mrs. A. F. McMahon is the
chairman.
Last evenmg the representatives of
the local circles and their friends
filled the Native Sons' Hall to tender
a reception to the grand guardian. The
degree teams of the nine circles were
ln attendance in gay uniforms and
entered the main aisle ln column of
two and at command opened rank and
with their spears formed a long arch
under which the guest of the evening
was led by the chairman of the cam
paign committee and these were fol
lowed by other grand officers and the
other members of the committee to
seats specially reserved for them.
After the presentation of a farce in
two acts, in which Miss C. Wehr, Miss
A. B. King and Captain Allie Perrip,
members of the order, and Edward
Healey and Walter Wood took part,
the chairman of the committee intro
duced the grand guardian neighbor,
who spoke at some length on the or
der throughout Pacific Coast Jurisdic
tion and in San Francisco ln particu
lar. She was followed by Grand
Clerk J. L. Wright, who spoke of the
protective feature of the order, and
then Grand Adviser Dr. Victory Der
rick spoke of the growth of the or
der. Each of the speakers was loud
ly applauded. After the
ing there was an order of eight dances.
HAYES OFFERS NO EVIDENCE
IN SUIT OF CHARLES MAIN
Case of 'Aged litigants Is Submitted
on Plaintiff's Showing and Briefs
WiU Be Filed.
The trial of the suit of Charles
Main against Thomas R. Hayes, his
friend and confidential employe for*
fifty years, came to an unexpected and
unusual termination in Judge Sea
well's court yesterday morning. The
testimony of the aged plaintiff oc
cupied only a few moments, he tell
ing of the latitude he had allowed
Hayes ln the drawing of checks, mak
ing Investments and attending to
other business affairs. The defense de
cided to offer no evidence, but Instead
of moving for a nonsuit, which, if
granted, would not have barred Main
from beginning another action, the at
torneys for Hayes submitted their
case on the plaintiffs evidence. Five
days were given in which to prepare
briefs.
Main knew nothing of the issuance
of the stock as bonus for the Stan
dard Portland Cement shares bought
by Hayes for him except by hearsay.
The evidence of W. G. Henshaw. who
sold the bonds, and who Is now in En.
rope, was not produced, and the de
fense thinks that the proof has not
been made that Hayes received the
$45,000 worth of stock for his em
ployer.

Two Past Passages.
The schooner Wawona and the British ship
"Inverness, both from this port, have won
1.. es on the list of sailing vessels with smart
passages to their credit. The schooner's repu
ior smart heels was earned over a much
shorter course than that traversed by the
square rigger, but both records are among the
best.' The Wawona has just arrived at South
Bend after a flying trip of four days from this
lÂ» rt-, and the Inverness, wheih left here De
cember 3, was reported yesterday off Sydney
Hfads, having crossed the Pacific ln forty-five
days. The British ship Inverclyde, which left
f.e're a month before the Inverness, was off
.Sydney.Heads In company with the Inverness,
having taken thirty days longer to reach the
came -point.

FATHER GREY'S MENTAL
AND PHYSICAL CONDITION
Rev. Patrick Powers Relates Inci
dents Tending to Prove Old Man's
Susceptibility to Influence.
The mental and physical condition
of Father P. J. Grey during the pe
riod of his relations with Dr. Thomas
R. Brennan was inquired into at yes
terday's hearing of the venerable
priest's suit against his erstwhile com
panion and medical adviser for the
recovery of 237,000. The witness was
Father Patrick Powers, who has been
associated with Father Grey in busi
ness and private affairs. The purpose
of his examination by Attorney M. C,
Haseett, in the plaintiff's behalf, was
to show that Father Grey, by reason,
of his age and infirmities, was sus
ceptible to the influences which it is
charged Brennan exercised to induce
the priest to withdraw his money
from the Hibernia Bank. Father
Powers knew of their associations
from the time Brennan took Father
Gray to Paso Robles In 1899 and
throughout their residence in this and
Alameda counties.
Father Powers testified that in some
of the matters in which he was inter
ested with Father Gray the latter had
forgotten the details. On St. Patrick's
day, 1903, Father Powers found
Father Grey unconscious and sup
posed he would die. He was Indig
nant to learn that the priest had not
been spiritually cared for and pro
cured this attention. Father Grey,
however, recovered, and Father Pow
ers advised him to consult another
physician than Brennan, and the ad
vice was taken. Other instances along
this line were related, and the exami
nation of Father Powers will be re
sumed this morning.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The pressure Is falling slowly along the
Oregon coast and conditions are becoming un- I
favorable for settled fair weather.
It is slightly cooler In the great valley of
California and warmer elsewhere.
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnight, January 18:
California north of TehachaplâShowers
Wedneeday; fresh southeast wind.
California south of TehachaplâCloudy
Wednesday; Heht north wind.
Nevada âCloudy Wednesday.
San Francisco and vicinityâCloudy, un
settled weather Wednesday, probably showers;
fresh southeast winds.
Los Angeles and vicinityâCloudy Wednee
day; light north wind.
Sacramento âCloudy, unsettled weather
Wednesday, possible rain by night; fresh
southeast winds.
FresnoâCloudy Wednesday; light south
winds. A. G. McADIE.
District Forecaster.
GRANDMOTHER PETITIONS
FOR CUSTODY OF CHILDREN
Mrs. Ella Chamberlain Makes Claim
in the Matter of Butler
Smith Estate.
Some changes were made and some i
new points added yesterday in the
three-cornered fight over the guard- I
ianship of the prospectively wealthy ;
children of the late Butler Smith. The !
hearing was begun before Judge Ker
rigan. The petition of Mrs. M. Find
lay, nurse of the two-year-old Ran
dolph Smith, was withdrawn ln view
of the stronger claims of relatives and
in place thereof the petition of Mrs.
Ella Chamberlain, grandmother of the
infants, was offered.
The widow had the first inning.
She said his estate is worth about
$45,000 and that of his former wife
about $100,000. The daughter, Gene
vieve, and the boy, Randolph, are in
her custody. Two witnesses testified
to her fitness to care for the children.
The little girl, who has just passed
her thirteenth year, took the stand
and in bright but quaint manner told
the Judge that she wanted "her
mama," the stepmother, to be her
guardian.
Butler Smith's will, made before his
third marriage, was produced at the
request of Mrs. Chamberlain's attor
ney. He had left $5000 to the grand
mother and gave her the custsdy of
the persons of the children, placing
the guardianship of their estate with
his son, Hal V. Smith of Chicago. This
son is one of the four children of
Smith's first wife. After a quarrel
with Mrs. Chamberlain Smith wrote
a codicil cutting out her bequest and
giving Hal Smith the guardianship of
the children.
The hearing will be resumed, this j
morning.
BROOKS TELLS ABOUT
WATER IN A CEMETERY
Spring Valley Owns a Part of Chinese
Graveyard in San Mateo County,
He Says.
In the Spring Valley Water Works
case yesterday before United States
Commissioner Heacock William
Brooks testified that the company 1
owned several small strips of land in
the Chinese Cemetesy in San Mateo
County. He said that the Crystal
Springs pipe line passed through the
cemetery and the company bought the
land for the purpose of preventing the
burial of any Chinese bodies suf- ,
flciently near to the pipe to cause {
pollution of the water.
Assistant City and County Attorney
John C. Partridge asked whether any
Chinese body was burled near enough
to the pipe to pollute the water, and
the witness replied in the negative. '
McCALLA WINS
THE DELEGATES
Members of BricklayerÃ , and
Masons' Convention Appre
ciate Kindness of Admiral
< â¢
COMMITTEES TO REPOET
1
Many Important Questions
to Come Up Are Expected
to Be Well Discussed
â¢
Much praise is being extended to j
Rear Admiral B. A. McCalla by the
delegates of the Bricklayers' and Ma
son's convention for the hospitable
manner in which he received them
! when they visited Mare Island Mon
\ day. They were shown the interesting
sights about the island. Much inter
est was manifested in the Russian
j transport Lena and several torpedo
1 boats. The submarine boat Pike was
; cruising about with the crew on top
j and was the center of attraction. On
I leaving the island % Secretary William
j Dobson, in behalf of the delegates,
j made a short speech, in which he said
; the members greatly appreciated the
j kindness shown them by so distin
guished a man. As the steamer was
, leaving the delegates were saluted
; from the island. At yesterday's scs
i sion of the convention the secretary
' was instructed to thank Rear Admiral
j McCalla in a suitable way for the
courtesies shown to them.
j Saturday evening the delegates were
guests at a ball given by the Oakland
union. Each delegate was presented
with a handsome souvenir.
The general routine work consumed
the time yesterday. Invitations from
Delegate Edward Gerity of Los An
geles and Delegate William Thayer of
Los Angeles were extended to the
delegates to stop off on their way go
ing home and be the guests of the
J unions of each of these cities.
Delegates from Minneapolis are
! working to have the convention select
that city for the next meeting place.
Resolutions congratulating Governor
| Alva Adams of Colorado on his elec
tion were adopted. They will be sent to
the Governor. It is expected that reports
will be heard from some committees at
to-day'a session, and a lively time Is
expected from now until the end of the
convention.

THE CONVICTION OF WYMAN.
THE courageous fight made by Mr. Fairfax WheeJan and the
Merchants' Association against the ballot-box stutters has not
ended with the conviction of Wyman. There are others who
will in due time be attended to. These crimes were committed at the
primary election last August. It is conceded by all students of our
polity that the primary election, the beginning of the operations by
which the people declare their will, is of more importance than the
general or municipal election which follows the primary. It is
through the primary that nominations for office are made, and when
criminal manipulators of that first process combine they can, by
corrupting the ballot*box, nominate apparently rival tickets which
offer to decent citizens no choice at all. Whichever ticket prevails
under such circumstances the grafters and corruptionists, united by
articles of criminal partnership, have their way âdespoil the public
treasury and the private citizen âand there is no redress.
Because of the importance of the primary and -its direct rela
tion to good government all citizens are regularly exhorted to vote
thereat âto vote rightâto see that no one votes on another's name
and that the exact ballots cast are tallied and counted. The press
has heretofore done its duty, for the most part, in this exhortation,
but, sad to say, with little effect. The stuffers of the primary, em
boldened by the lack of watchfulness and able often to drive the
occasional decent citizen away from the polls by threat and insult,
have had their way, and it has been a foul way. Under these cir
cumstances there has developed a system of official winking at this
form of fraud. This was to have been expected. When it is possible
to corrupt the primary the official authority that generates in a cor
rupt ballot rises no higher than its source. It is created in the
crimes at which it winks and the criminals feel an immunity that
nerves them to further excesses.
At the August primary Mr. Fairfax Wheelan and a few other
citizens of courage and determination concluded that their duty at
the primary was more than perfunctory. They took it seriously and
seriously entered upon its discharge. They found this far from a
pleasant personal experience, for they were treated as interlopers
who interfered with what seemed to have become more than a pre
scriptive right to stuff ballot-boxes and falsify returns. In the face
Of much personal inconvenience and some personal danger Mr.
Wheelan stood his ground and secured the testimony which has
started one stuffer toward the penitentiary and has set other crimi
nals shaking in their shoes. The work of prosecution has only be
gun and it will be followed up with energy, discretion and all the
resources of the Merchants' Association.
But the work should not stop there. If the decency of the city,
represented by the Merchants' Association, can convict ballot-box
stuffers and protect the purity of the primary, it has upon it the duty
to go further and create a public administration no part of which, not
even the least, will be found winking at crime and encouraging crim
inals. This is the further and greater work that lies ahead. When
every public expression of official authority is in the spirit that has
been shown In the prosecution of the ballot frauds there will be no
such frauds to prosecute, for even criminals will not take risks ex
cept in the hope of gain, and from such an administration as may be
they cannot hope for any advantage.
The whole incident, from the day that Mr. Wheelan stood his
ground against difficulty to the verdict of the jury, is an enfranchise
ment of the decent, self-respecting citizens of San Francisco. It is
the first time in years that they have been made conscious of their
~ power. In this awakening their duty is revealed. It is that of constant
watchfulnessâthat eternal vigilance which is the price of good gov
ernment. It illuminates the pathway to this achievement, for there
is only one. If two be taken the result will not be reached. The
people who now, irrespective of their partisan affiliation, feel the
common inspiration of what has been accomplished must carry that
common inspiration forward in a common purpose in the .future. If
they go up one path together at its farther end lies good govern
ment. But if they divide and follow two paths they will exhaust them
selves uselessly. In that case the common inspiration will evaporate
and the last state of the city will be worse than the first.
Under the existing charter of this city there is less need for mere
partisan division than ever and greater need for a combination of the
virtue and civic pride .of all the people. Some of the depths which
reckless administration may sound are known by experience, and
this knowledge is a bugle call to such watchfulness as has been
shown in prosecuting the ballot-box stuffers. It also calls to a
further and higher duty, in which men must forget their general
political differences and divisions and stand together in a common
purpose, the highest that can inspire a good citizen to do his duty.
The Merchants' Association at its last annual banquet expressed
its high purpose in utterances that have drawn the attention of the
whole country. They raised great expectations far abroad. Let
these be realized, and let us have no lame and impotent conclusion of
purposes so worthy and so worthily expressed.
THE LIVE STOCK ASSOCIATION.
THE meeting of the National Live Stock Association in Denver
resulted in the disruption of that organization. The cattlemen
withdrew and have formed a separate cattlemen's association.
This is probably a wise movement. The issue in the association for
years has been the treatment of the grazing lands on the public do
main. The cattlemen have wished those ranges put under a leasehold
control, so that all land that is fit for grazing only can have its forage
protected, and not destroyed as it is by its use in common. But the
cattlemen have lacked coherence and unity of effort. They have
given a feeble support in Congress to the leasing policy, and the
sheepmen have given it a vigorous and intelligent opposition. The
result has been that the sheepmen have forced the cattlemen off the
range over a considerable part of the public domain. The cattle
men have made up for their lack of vigor before Congress by un
lawful reprisals on the range. They have tried to hold it against
the sheepmen with Winchesters and poison. In this range war there
has been considerable loss of life and a great loss of property, for
sheep have been killed and cattle have been starved by thousands.
If the cattlemen will now go by themselves, form their own asso
ciation and, refraining from violence, will plan a wise campaign be
fore â¢ Congress, the country will be'brought to see the wisdom of
preserving the grazing lands which can never be used for anything
else. When this campaign is wisely entered upon, it is within the
possibilities that a large percentage of the sheepmen will join in it
and we may have once more peace, prosperity and preservation on
the range.

GREER, DISCIPLINARIAN
ROXANA'S 'dog was an ill-condi
tioned brute. He was a surly
buU pup that had never met his
master, and delighted ln making
it most uncomfortable for those who
sought Roxana's company. He was
not often docile, even with her, but she
was so proud of his blue ribbon and
ugliness that she endeavored to hide
his delinquencies. Nevertheless, there
were times when she would not have
gone near him for the world.
One day he snarled at her before
Goddard Greer, and that was the be
ginning- of trouble. Greer was a col
lege-bred athlete, who feared nothing
on earth, except, perhaps, Roxana. En
raged, he seized thejuckless prize-win
ner by the collar and bore him to the
i hallway, where he administered a
j strong argument, after which he turned
the amazed animal loose down the
! back stairs. Goddard returned to the
parlor arranging his cuffs, but other
wise as usual. He stopped short as he
faced Roxana, who looked like an out
raged empress.
"A truly fine performance," she de
clared scathingly. "There is your ring!
I never wish to see you again!"

DEAR YOUTH, a comrade wild as sweet thou art;
Thou lurest me to moods of glad excess.
When joys on joys so closely round me press
They almost crush my lightly beating heart.
Inspired by thee, my eager fancies start
To quickened life, and tempt me to digress
The old familiar paths, to seek the stress
Of ways untried, or else to freely dart
A course as careless as a wanton wind.
I love these moods that through my pulses fly,
I love their madness; on that day I find
Their dear delights have passed away, shall I
Lamentâor closer clasp what stays behind?
The joy that lives In thee can never die.
âCora Roche Howland In Chicago Journal.
MONOCLES FOR WOMEN
THE LATEST FAD.
No loncer is the lorgnette the badge
of the New York grande dame. The
newest fad in this direction is the
monocle for women, says the World of
that city. Of course, the woman of
fashion could not risk her "make-up"
by sticking the glass in her eye. In
stead, she has the lens set in gold and
holds it to her eye. Mrs. Oliver H. P.
Belmont invariably uses a monocle at
the opef*a, and hers Is set in a gold
filigree frame. Poising the single glass,
she surveys the other women in the
parterre. Mrs. J. Fred, Tarns, famed
as the mother of the beautiful Violet
Cruger, also uses the monocle on all
occasions. The glass dangles from a
chain, set with pearls. Few of the
younger women have taken up the
monocle, because it implies impaired
eyesight. There is Mrs. Astor, for in
stance, who never uses any optical aid
in public except her opera glasses.
A TALE OF A TUB.
The two pretty American girls had
met two delightful Englishmen on
the way across and had given a cor
dial invitation, warmly seconded by
their mother, to Sir Charles and his
friend to visit them at their country
home. *
One day a message came saying the
two men would arrive that afternoon.
The family was thrown into a fever
of excitement, and many plans of en
tertainment for their guests were sug
gested and abandoned. It was finally
decided that as Englishmen are no
toriously fond of a "tub," and their
guest* were coming directly from the
train, they should first be invited to
take a bath. After that the hostess
would rely upon the inspiration of the
moment.
The young men arrived promptly
and after some demurring were hur
ried off to the bathroom. In about an
hour they emerged and went immedi
ately to their hostess, saying, "We are
sorry to leave so soon, but we only
came to make a call, and our train
leaves ln fifteen minutes." âMary C.
Frankfurter in January Lippincott's.
The Universal Trolley.
The bridging of gaps in the network
of trolley lines between New York and
Boston has gone on more rapidly than
was anticipated and now the last open
ing, that between New Haven and Wal
lingford, has been closed. As yet the
service is disconnected and except for
the leisurely inclined tourist too slow
for practical use, but there is no reason
why a through service could not be
maintained that would offer as many
inducements to travelers as the steam
road service. The Philadelphia-New
York network was completed last sum
mer. Slowly but surely the entire At
lantic seaboard is being bound together
by the universal trolley.

Heroically Roxana left the car, and
walked north. She had never been
alone on the street so late, and, re
membering the distance, the darkness
and the loneliness of the blocks, she
was really alarmed. She did not fear

ed the sound of footsteps behind, con

shp hurried up the deserted thorough
fare, it seemed to her that some one
followed. If she hastened, the pur
suing footsteps quickened. When she
slackened speed, the other did the
same. It frightened her, and she be
came breathless. With the passing
minutes her terror gained. She be
gan to run. Then the dread became a
certainty. The other one would soon
overtake her. What if that negro had
seen her gold purse?

at her.
"I guess that will do for one night,
he remarked coldly. "Come over to
the drug store, while I phone for a
cab."
Completely unnerved Roxana fol
lowed. The reaction had been sudden.
She tried to remember what they had
quarreled about, but couid only feel
relief in his presence. Greer ordered
the cab. then went outside to wait.
"Come," he commanded tersely on
its arrival.
Gratefully Roxana obeyed, then as
he was about to close the door, she
found her voice. "Don't leave me."
"I've no intention of doing so." And
he mounted by the -river. When they
reached her home, Roxana's eyes
were suspiciously red.
"Well." said Greer. The bull pup
sniffed respectfully at his boots. Greer

tree â' " began Greer teasingly.
"Hush, hush!" whispered Roxana,

I never really believed â"
"Of course not," he agreed prompt
ly. Then as his arms tightened about

TO YOUTH.
LEAP YEAR PROPOSAL
ON A TREE.
John Crouse. 5310 Heiskill street,
Germantown, is the recipient of a
strange leap year proposal, which ar
rived from Maine tied tightly to the
trunk of a huge Christmas tree, and if
the young woman in the land of
Christmas trees be in earnest the
strange communication will probably
culminate in a marriage, says a Phil
adelphia dispatch to the Chicago In
ter Ocean.
Crouse purchased the tree several
days ago, and, upon trimming the
branches, discovered a neatly folded
note tied fast to the trunk.
The envelope was addressed: "To
whoever may get this tree, with
wishes for a merry Christmas." The
letter was written In a flowing hand,
as follows:
"I am a young lady, 20 years old,
and am desirous of becoming the wife
of a good, loving man, who will pro
vide a home in some large city. I
cut this tree down myself, and with
every stroke of the ax I wished that
the tree might make a merry Christ
mas and bring a good husband to
me."
The name was signed and a post
office address of a small village in Pe
nobscot County was given. Crouse
immediately answered the note, ask
ing for further details of the young
womatffl matrimonial views and is
eagerly awaiting developments.
Editor The Call: It may be re
peated as a basis for a thought or
two upon the cause of earthquakes
that the foundations of the earth, con
sisting of the solid strata upon which
the surface rests, have evidently not
yet become permanently fixed. If
some of the temblors which have oc
curred recently at ' San Francisco
were caused by heavy blastings, and
others also recent seismic shakes,
why may not some of the quakes be
attributed to a settling and displace
ment of the underlying rock founda
tions by reason of the stupendous
weight of the millions of tons of ma
sonry piled up on the surface in the
massive buildings constructed of late
years on the northern portion of the
San Francisco peninsula? We may not
know what gravitation is. but weight
is clearly one of the causes of that
force. W. S. HARLOW.

A pretty luncheon was given yester
day by Miss Gertrude Palmer at her
home on Jackson street. Covers were
laid for a dozen maids, and the table
most attractive in soft shades of pink,

WILL RESTRICT
SALE OF POISON
Ragan Proposes Law Prohib
iting- Purchase of Carbolic
Acid, Without Prescription
*
Health Officer D. F. Ragan yester
day decided to recommend to the
Board of Health that the Board of
Supervisors be petitioned to enact an
ordinance prohibiting the sale of car
bolic acid by druggists without a pres
cription. Dr. Ragan is desirous of re
ducing the fearful mortality from car
bolic acid taken with suicidal intent.
Dr. Ragan has ascertained that last
year there were forty-four cases of
carbolic acid poisoning treated at the
Central Emergency Hospital. Of these
sixteen died. Ragan has also discov
ered that many cases of carbolic acid
prisoning are not taken to the Emer
gency Hospital. Dr. Ragan is of the
opinion that a law requiring a physi
cian's prescription will lessen to a
great extent the mortality.
Another matter unon which Ragan
will file a report is the quality of the
gas furnished in this city. He asserts
that more, deaths result from asphyxia
tion in this city in proportion to the
population than any other city in the
wcrld and ascribes the cause to the
poisonous quality of the carbon monox
ide in the gas. In this category acci
dental deaths are also included.
Chemical tests will at once be made
to show the poisonous character of the
gas and publicity will be given to the
results of the analysis so the people
will be warnecKof the danger existing
therein.
DUNSMUIR CASE BEFORE
COMMISSIONER BENNETT
Friends of Legatee Visit Court, but
Testimony FnzzlesrThem and They
Depart Unenlightened.
The struggle for the Dunsmuir mil
lions is still occupying the attention of
Commissioner Bennett at the British
Consulate. Attorney J. J. Dwyer was
the only witness again at both sessions
yesterday.
He went on in the same line of tes- !
timony as on the two preceding days.
Yesterday was his third day as a wit- ;
ness.
Attorney S. Wheeler continued the
examination. -All his interrogations
were based on lines to prove what the
law Is in California in reference to the ,
execution of wills and the conclusive- \
ness of probate decrees.
Expert Dwyer has so far cited over
50 cases relating to probate procedure,
and he is not through yet. It had
been expected that Attorney Wheeler
would finish his examination of the
witness yesterday, but at 4:45 p. m. he '
was still going on with his questions,
so Consul Bennett adjourned the court
until this morning at 10:30. Immedi- '
ately following the direct examination !
to-day the attorneys for the plaintiff
will take up the cross examination.
Edna Wallace Hopper was not pres- j
ent at either of the sessions yester- i
day.
Late yesterday afternoon three men j
presented themselves at the Consulate
and said they had been friends of 1
James Dunsmuir and they wanted to
"hear the case." They were admitted, i
The small room was already filled with
those immediately Interested in the i
case, so the three visitors held up the !
side walls for a while. The method I
of procedure was evidently not what
they had expected. After listening to I
the citations of Expert Dwyer for a
ffcW minutes they turned to each other
and asked, "What's It all about any- j
way ?" As no light was thrown on the j
subject, they slowly filed out of court ,
and are probably still wondering.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, JANUARY 18, 1905.
GLAD TIDINGS
FAIL TO COME
Arthur Piper Waits Vainly
in the County Jail for
Order Setting Him Free
JUDGES DENY PETITION
Supreme Court Thinks That
Shipping Man Should Be
Confined Until He Pays
Arthur Leroy Piper must remain in
jail until lie decides to pay his wife
$150 on back alimony. The Supreme
Court has canvassed the circumstances
in the case and yesterday Piper's pe
tition for release on habeas corpus was
returned with "Writ denied" written
across the back of the document. The
attorneys for the prisoner were so con
fident they would secure their client's
release that they affixed an order for
the writ to issue leaving vacant the
places for the date and the signatures
of the Justices. The court's action in
dicates that the petition did not state
facts sufficient to warrant an investi
gation of the case.
While divorce proceedings were pend
ing Piper was directed to pay for his
wife's maintenance. Last June he was
shy $625 on his payments, but up to
January 6 he succeeded in getting con
tinuances. Then Judge Sloss ordered
him to separate himself from $150 or
go to Jail. Piper pleaded poverty, lack
of employment and general financial
debility, but to no avail, for Judge Sloss
issued an order for his commitment.
Deputy Sheriff McManus took Piper
into custody at the latter's home at
2921 Jackson street and removed him
to the County Jail.
The prisoner was not greatly affect
ed at the time as he thought his at
torneys could quickly make him free
again. Other Superior Judges were
asked to release Piper and then the
matter was taken to the Supreme
Court. Saturday afternoon the prison
er's lawyers attempted to persuade the
Justices to take action, but none of
them would consider the writ. Much
to the disappointment of Piper he was
forced to remain in Jail over Sunday.
As the case now stands, he must tar
ry behind the bars until he Is ready to
settle.
Petitions in Insolvency.
Martin H. Stephens, a carpenter re
siding in San Jose, filed a petition in
insolvency yesterday in the United
States District Court. Joined with
him in the petition was Sadie E.
Stephens, a housewife. Their liabil
ities are $409 and they have no as
sets. The petition was rejected be
cause It was not drawn up in proper
form. E. W. Allen, retail grocer of
San Jose, filed a petition also. He
owes $12,427 and has $2050 assets.
His principal creditors are the Bank
of Campbell, $2600; M. F. Allen of
Campbell, $3387, and Lillie F. Shaw,
$2901.

ADVERTISEMENTS.
Reliable Directory of Society People
Just Issued.
Charles C. Hoag has just taken from the press the San Francisco Blue
Book for the season of 1906. It has a blue binding, illuminated with gold.
The contents are printed in colors and arranged in departments repre
senting the leading families ln San Francisco and one hundred suburban
towns, giving the names, addresses and reception days. The Men's Clubs
with complete lists of members, are given. Also diagrams of the Thea
ters, personnel of the Press, Ladies' Shopping Guide. Street and Avenue
Guide, giving the car line to any desired number.
As for its intrinsic worth, the fact that it is issued with the official
Indorsement of the California Federation of Women's Clubs is a sufficient
guarantee of Its reliability.
Since the women of California have found that they have so many in
terests in common this book is a necessity and a comfort It puts the
women of the uttermost parts of the State ln communication with each
other. In addition to the names and addresses of 10,000 women in this
book, the date of organization and the times of meeting of each club are
indicated.
Address CHAS. C. HO AG, Publisher,
320 Sansome St., Room 47,
Telephone Main 1760. Son Francisco.
POPULAR RESIDENT
OF MARYSVILLE
DIES AT HIS HOME
FORMER SHERIFF DAN DONAHOE 1
OF YUBA COUNTY WHO DIED
IN THIS CITY. I
4 +
Daniel Donahoe, one of the most
popular citizens of Marysville, died
in that city yesterday after a short
illness. Marysville is in mourning
over the loss, for "Dan," as he was
generally called, has done much for
the city.
He was foremost in advocating im
provements, and his purse was always
open to the needy. In all public
affairs his opinion carried weight, and
his advice was sought on all matters
of public importance. He served as
Sheriff of Yuba County from 1896 to
1898.
Mr. Donahoe was born in Montreal,
Canada, on June 6, 1842. As a youth
he came to California and made his
home In Marysville, where he en
tered the employ of Hon. P. C, Slat
tery- In 1881 he founded the resort
known as the "Mint." The place is
known to thousands. Mr. Donahoe
leaves a widow and six children. The
deceased was the brother of Mrs.
Kate Hughes of Montreal.
DIES SUDDENLY WHILE
SEATED ON STREET CAR
Mrs. W. W. Savcrcool Stricken Down
in Sight of Her Husband. Who
Has Just Said Goodby.
Just after her husband had placed
her In a seat on a Kearny street car
at Market street yesterday afternoon,
Mrs. W. W. Savercool sank back sud
denly and died. Her husband was look
ing at her in bidding goodby as she
swooned apparently, and was quickly
at her side again. He gathered her
in his arms but she gasped in death
without a word to him. The body was
carried to a drug store, and a doctor
said either apoplexy or heart disease
was the cause. Mrs. Savercool was
50 years of age.
She and her husband had come re
cently to San Francisco to live, after
having lived in Plumas County many
years, where Savercool was engaged in
mining.
MRS. SELBY'S
FINAL DECREE
Wife of Minister Who Shot
at Judge Hebbard Comes
in Person to Ask Entry
DEPLOKES THE DEED
Says Husband Called Upon
Her on Day of Shooting
and Was Denied Admission
Theresa B. Selby yesterday severed
the last legal tie binding her to Isaac
Selby, the Australian minister, who is
now on trial for his attempt upon the
life of Superior Judge Hebbard. She
appeared personally in court and re
quested the entry of the final decree
of divorce in accordance with the in
terlocutory decree granted by Judge
Hebbard on January 15 last year. It
was the grievance Selby nursed over
this decree which Caused him to shoot
at the Judge. Mrs. Selby said to
Clerk J. J. Groom that she deplored
the trouble which had come to the
Judge through her case, and she also
said that on the morning of the shoot
ing Selby had come to her door and
knocked, but she refused to admit
him. She expressed the belief that
he might have killed her then.
Upon the evidence of Annie B. Hig
glns, corroborated in a circumstantial
way by bell and elevator boys em
ployed at the Hotel Inverness, Judge
Hebbard yesterday granted her a di
vorce from Dr. Theodore Shelton Hig
gins, dentist and former proprietor of
the hotel. Mrs. Higgins said that she
had gone to the country on a vacation
and, returning unexpectedly, found
articles of feminine apparel in her
husband's rooms, which did not be
long to her wardrobe. Mrs. Higgins
said that during her husband's attend
ance at the dental college she worked
ln a store in order to support him.
Judge Seawell granted a divorce to
Delia Stobener from John W. Stoben
er, superintendent of Cne Home of
Peace Cemetery, on a cross-complaint
charging crueky. Stobener first sued
and accused his wife of abusing him
self and the patrons of the burial
place. She denied the allegations and
said that he had mistreated her in
many Ways, including beatings, and at
last had deserted her. On the latter
pleading the divorce, was granted.
In the suit of Dr. G. K. Hubbell
against Harriet Hubbell for divorce,
the wife yesterday filed a sensational
answer. She denies his charges of
cruelty and says she never maintained
an espionage over his affairs. In her
own defense she says he was very at
tentive to Miss May F. Hallett, and
she believes that he promised to mar
ry her. The wife says that she assist
ed him in his practice and attended
to his correspondence. In the course
of this duty she opened several let
ters which she quotes in her answer.
One of them, signed "Nell," is full of
endearing terms and calls the doctor
"a fascinating, charming, bewitching
darling."
Dr. William M. McLaren, whose
wife, May E. â McLaren, accused him
of neglect and cruelties, including his
compelling her to walk eighty-six
blocks to. her home, yesterday filed
specific denials to alt of her accusa
tions.
Judge Kerrigan granted a divorce
to Eunice P. Cushman from Charles
Cushman for cruelty, with $30 a
month alimony and $1000 of com
munity property; to Margaret R. Gan
non from Joseph J. Gannon, cruelty;
Judge Hebbard to Kittle M. Clancy
from Robert K. Clancy, neglect;
Judge Murasky to James P. Daly from
Mary Ellen Daly, cruelty.
Suits for legal separation were be
gun by John R. Whalen against
Amanda Whalen, desertion; Edward
Ferrel against Alice Ferrel, cruelty;
Maggie Gunther against William C.
Gunther, desertion; Ellen M. Brodies
against James Brodies, neglect; Goldie
Church against Royal Church, cruelty
and neglect.

CENTRAL PACIFIC RAILWAY COMPANY
' FIRST REFUNDING MORTGAGE BONDS- -
Notice is hereby given that, pursuant to the
provision of article eight of the mortgage
from this company to the Central Trust Com
pany of New York, trustee, dated August 1,
1890, this company has set apart out of UM
net income derived by it from the lines of
railroad therein mortgaged the sum of twen
ty-five thousand dollars in trust to be used
to redeem said bonds, and that bonds Issued
under said mortgage will be redeemed there
with. Bids are hereby invited for the sur
rendei of such bonds at prices to be named
by the bidders, to the amount of $26.67"
In the sirklng fund. Such bids should b.
presented to this company at its office. 120
Broadway, ln the city of New York, on or
before the 14th day of February, 1905, at 12
o'clock noon, and should be indorsed "Bias
for Surrender of Central Pacific Railway
Company First Refunding Bonds."
CENTRAL PACIFIC RAILWAY COMPANY.
By E. H. HARRIMAN. President.
New York, January 16. 1905.

PACKERS VOTE
TO SELL BONDS
'Authorize an Issue That
Amounts to $2,000,000
to 3leet New Conditions
ALSO INCREASE STOCK
â  S s
Add $2,500,000 to Total
Capitalization, Raising the
Figures to $7,500,000
The stockholders of the Alaska Pack
ers' Association voted yesterday to in
crease the capital stock of the corpo
ration from $5,000,000 to $7,500,000. An
issue of bonds amounting to $2,000,000
I was also authorized. At the close of
; the meeting it was announced that the
' bonds had all been sold and that more
i than $1,000,000 of the new stock had
I been subscribed for.
Henry F. Fortmann was elected
i president; D. Drysdale and A. B. Brad
j ford, vice presidents; C. W. Dorr, sec
retary and general counsel, and Isaao
I Llebes, treasurer. ' The following wars
[ elected directors: Henry F. Fortmann,
IW. B. Bradford, D. Drysdale, Isaao
Llebes. Francis Cutting, F. B. Ander
son, William H. Crocker, F. L. Lip
man. William L. Gerstle, Louis Sloss,
M. H. Hecht. An annual report was
filed by Secretary Dorr, in which ths)
general situation was described as fol
lows:
Ths year 1904 will long be remembered aa
the most trying and unremuneratlve season is
the history of the association. It opened with
large unsold stocks of canned salmon ln pack
ers' hands, of which the association held at
least Its full share. This came about as ths
culmination of a number of unfortunate cir
cumstances resulting ln thoroughly demoralis
ed and ur.responstve markets throughout ths
world. Among the chief causes which con
tributed to this unhappy situation may he
mentioned an unnatural and purely speculative
promoters' boom ln the salmon business; aa
enormous overproduction of canned salmon dur
ing the three precedlrg years, especially ln 1901,
when the coast output was abnormally In
creased from 3,000,000 cases to 5.000,000; and
the attitude of certain salmon packers whs
were either going through bankruptcy or foe
other causes became persistent price cutters.
By reason of an unprecedented ice Jam la
the Bering Sea the 1904 pack was made mors
costly than that of any previous year. Ths
burden of carrying previous packs into tha
year IPO4 end consequent reduction of values
has been enormous, but by no means disas
trous to the association. But for the fact that
very large sums had recently been expended
for betterments and improvements to the plants
and fleet, even this burden would have rested
lightly, but, coming as tt did at a most In
opportune time. It has produced serious hard
ships to those charged with the preservation
of the property of the association and ths
management of Its affairs.
It is, however, exceedingly gratifying to note
at this Juncture that the clouds seem to be
! rapidly breaking and ln due course of time
I the association may be expected to regain its
j old-time vigor and strength and resume Its
| commercial dignity and Importance as one of
the foremost industrial corporations of ths
j Pacific Coast.
Subsequent to the proceedings Presl
-1 dent Fortmann said that the bond ls
| sue, which will extend over a period of
j fifteen years, will furnish money to
j carry on the business that has been
] built up. Formerly the salmon was
â¢ sold as soon as it was packed to
some large dealer, who resold it to the
trade. The trade conditions have
changed. Jobbers at New Orleans or
other points do not desire to buy from
New York, but to deal with the pack
ers. Stocks have to be carried.
A large fleet of vessels has been cre
ated that has cost as much as one
! third of the old capitalization. The
; company decided that it was wiser to
i provide the proper amount of money to
| meet the new conditions and accord
\ ingly bonds were voted by the stock
holders. During the past year the gross
profit was $109,198. The reserve funds
j amount to $2,104,103. The association
I now owns eighty-one vessels. During
! the past year ten consecutive divl
! dends of $1 a share were paid, and two
monthly dividends of 75 cents a share,
aggregating $552,000.
Pilots' Demurrer Sustained.
Judge Graham yesterday sustained
the demurrer of F. W. Jordan and
twenty other bay pilots ln the case of
Catherine Brady, who sued for $50,009
damages for the death of her husband.
Thomas H. Brady, second engineer of
the steamer Rio de Janeiro, who was
drowned when the ship sunk off Fort
Point, February 22, 1901. The point
made in the demurrer was that a pilot
is a public officer, and that an asso
ciation of pilots could not perform tha
functions of the public officer. There
fore the defendants could not be held
liable for the faults of one of their
number.

Chicago Grain and Produce.
CHICAGO, Jan. 17.âThe wheat market had
a weak undertone throughout the entire ses
sion. Initial quotations on May were off %c
to %o%c at $1 15 to $1 15%0l 154. Scattered
selling by commission houses and pit traders
was the Immediate cause of the opening de
cline. "The chief factor responsible for the sell
ing pressure was a break of almost a penny
ln the price of wheat at Liverpool. The weak
ness of the English market was In turn said
to be due to cheaper Argentine offerings, In
dicating an increase In shipments from that
country. After the first rush of selling had
subsided the market here became somewhat
firmer. The faot that Northwest receipts etlll
continued to arrive ln decreased quantities had
a tendency to check the general desire to sell.
Total primary receipts also were much smaller
than for the corresponding day a year ago,
| and this had a slightly bullish effect. Follow
ing fair trading by the leader of the bulls,
pit traders made moderate purchases, result
ing ln May advancing to $1 154Â©i 16%. Weak
ness of cash wheat at all the principal cen
ters, however, had a depressing influence on
speculative trading, and the market soon
sagged off again. The close was easy, with
May at $1 15%.
The corn market was rather essy esrly in
the session, but buying by shorts resulted In
a firmer undertone during the latter part of
the session. May opened a shade to 4c lower
at 44% c to 44%Â©44% c, sold up to 46c. and
closed at 44%@44%c.
A decrease of half a million bushels In local
stocks for the week had a strengthening ef
fect on the oats market. May opened un
changed to 4c lower at 30%Â©31 c, sold un to
814 c and closed at 3140314 c.
Provisions were dull and a shade easier At
the close May pork was off 24c Lard and
ribs were each unchanged.

LONDON, Jan. 17.âThere was a large at
tendance at the opening of the first series of
tha 1905 wool auction sales to-day. Several

districts were light and carried-over stock sold

â¢ STOCK MARKET.
A Heavy and Interesting Day on the
Bond Exchange.
Yesterday was a heavy and Interesting day
en the Stock and Bond Exchange, sales being
large and varied and fluctuations numerous.
The sugars continued to attract the most at
tention and were still higher, though ln the
alternoon some wavering appeared, giving
several a reacting aspect. Tbe high prices of
the day were: Hawaiian Commercial $83,
Puauhau $25 12H. Hutchinson $18 25, Honokaa
$22. Onomea $35 and Makawell $37. The close
In these stocks was easy.
After the sugar group Alaska Packers was
the next interesting stock, being Very sensitive
owing to the afternoon meeting of the stock
holders called to ratify the proposed plan of
reorganization already published. The stock
acid off to $86 50, a further decline of $2 75.
Another Interesting security was the 6 per cent
bonds of the North Shore Railroad, which
defaulted on their interest along last October.
They began to rise last week, selling up to
par, and yesterday rose to $101, falling back
somewhat, however, at the close. The total
sales of bondi were $87,000. There was con
siderable doing In Gas and Electric at a slight
advance to $50 62H.
The brokers say that the current activity in
local stocks and bonds Is more or less due to
the desire of banks, large corporations and
individual capitalists to Invest their surplus
funds In view of the forthcoming tax assess
ment day in March. Of course dividends also
cut a large figure in this buying demand, as
in the case of the sugar stocks. Again, a
good many stocks have been rather low and
shreyd operators have lately started In to buy
from an investment or speculative point of
view. All these are factors in ths current
activity In local securities.
There was no particular change ln the min
ing â locks, trading being very fair and to

PAIL NEED NOT
MARRY PAULINE
Mrs. Hulse's Breach of Prom
ise Suit Is Defeated in
Judge Kerrigan's Court
WOMAN POPS QUESTION
Ligda Testifies That He Was
Often Asked to Wed by
One of Prior Experience
"Woman proposes and man exposes,"
is a revision of the old axiom justified
by the proceedings and developments
of a breach of promise suit tried and
decided yesterday afternoon in Judge
Kerrigan's court. Pauline Hulse, a
Russian, twice married and once di
vorced, demanded $50,000 from Paul
Ligda, son of a former Greek Consul,
and a student at the University of
California, with literary aspirations,
because he had promised to marry her,
but had failed to take her to the altar.
Judge Kerrigan believed the young
man's sworn statement that he had
never asked the plaintiff to be his wife,
but had said "Nay, nay, Pauline" to
her repeated proposals, and judgment
for the defendant was entered accord-
lngly.
Mrs. Hulse told her story on the
stand. Llgda, she said, had been at
tentive when she was yet the wife of
Charles Hulse, a lodging-house keeper,
and promised to wed her when she was
divorced. After the separation, she
said, she went to Russia and Ligda
followed her with letters. She told of
meetings after her return from the
Cxar's domain, and in particular told
of a conversation on Kearny street,
wherein she asked Paul when he was
going to marry her, and he replied:
"Thursday or Friday, and then you will
be my wife."
This part of her evidence was corrob
orated by Mrs. May Jacobsen, who said
she was close behind the pair and
heard the question and answer. Her
seven-year-old daughter was along,
and she was called as a witness, the
defense waiving her taking the oath
and all questions as to her competency
to testify. Although it happened near
ly two years ago, the little girl told the
same story her mother told.
Then Ligda took the stand. His pro
nunciation is not of the plainest, but he
denied that he had ever requested Mrs.
Hulse to marry him, either before or
after her divorce; denied that he wrote
to her in Russia or elsewhere, and de
nied the stories of the meetings after
her return. Then came the man's ex
pose. "She was quite stuck on me,
and used to run after me all the time.
She forced herself on me, and I had to
read her Russian papers which were
sent to her. She always hinted that
she would make me a fine wife; that
she had been twice married and had
lots of experience in that way. I had
no position, and was a student at the
university, so I could not marry- She
came out several times and asked me
to marry her."
Lilgda's attorney, James H. Moyer,
attempted to question Ms client upon
Mrs. Hulse's moral reputation, but
Judge Kerrigan ruled him out. They
offered in evidence the decree wherein
Hulse was granted a divorce on the
ground of his wife's Intemperance.
The case was submitted without ar
gument, and Judge Kerrigan withheld
his decision until the parties had left
the courtroom.
Says He Was Beaten and Robbed.
James Riley, a miner from Mendo
cino County, complained to Police
men Duffy and Collins on Grant ave
nue, near Sutter street, early yester
day morning that he had been beaten
by two men and robbed.of $95. He
pointed out Joseph McElligott and
Robert Murray as the men who had
robbed him. They were arrested and
locked up in "the tanks" at the City
Prison pending developments. Riley
was under the influence of liquor and
was booked for being drunk. He has
a pair of black eyes. He was re
leased yesterday.

FELL THIRTY FEET FROM STAGING
B. E. Finch, a carpenter, fell from a height
of thirty feet from a staging at a building on
Twentieth and Harrison street* yesterday aft
ernoon. Dr. Carl G. Wilson retained him at
the Emergency Hospital, fearing he may have
sustained a fracture of the skull.
