| 216 POST THIRD >STRBET
RAPID GROWTH
OF METROPOLIS
President Newhall of Cham
ber of Commerce Makes
a Series of Comparisons
POET GAINS IN TBADE
Large Figures Are Needed
to Indicate the Import
ance of City as Center
At the annual meeting of the Cham
ber of Commerce yesterday reports
were submitted by President Newhall,
Secretary Scott and William M. Bun
ker, the Washington. D. C, represent
ative of the chamber. Incidental to
President Newhall's report ) he re
viewed the commercial conditions of
San Francisco. He said in part:
During the> year 1004 the total export trade,
by sea, from San Francisco ln value amounted
to more than $55,000,000, and the products oÂ£
California were more largely represented than
in any previous year. Notwithstanding the
heavy decrease In wheat export, owing to light
crops, this was an Increase ln our export busi
ness. The development of trade with the
Orient has passed all expectations and the
character of the business Indicates that it is
of a permanent nature.
The gain ln the Oriental trade over the year
1903 was $7,625,849. Exclusive of this there
were exports to Asia to other than the so
called Oriental countries amounting to nearly
$4,000,000. The year records, in addition to
this, for the first time four single out-going
cargoes of merchandise, namely, the Algoa,
October 29, whose cargo was valued at $1,
--470,000; Manchuria, on November 19, $1,768,
--000; Aztec, on November 25. $1,033,000, and
the Mongolia, on December 28, $1,645,000, and
this did not include the treasure, but simply
the merchandise.
The total import trade by sea during the
year 1904 was more than $43,000,000. Com
paring this with our previous year It shows
a nin of over $6,000,000.
The prosperity of San Francisco is shown by
the steady, substantial growth of the banks.
The commercial banks, with resources in ex
cess of $126,000,090 and deposits of over $70,
--000,000, amount to thirty-three. Eleven sav
ings banks, whose resources aggregate more
than $175,000,000 and their enormous deposits
of some $160,000,000, point to the general pros
perity of the city.
Say the city's population is 460,000. This
shows an average per capita of over $355 on
savings deposit account, a very 'â¢algh average.
The national banks, seven in number, have
resources of over $60,000,000 and deposits of
more than $25,000,000. The fifty-one banks
ln all ln the city count their deposits at over
$250,000,000.
The bank clearings of 1904 ln San Francisco
aggregated $1,534,631,137, being an Increase
over 1903 of $14,430,435. This increase aver
ages more than $1,200,000 a month. The bulk
of this business was gained during the last
half of the present year. San Francisco's
clearings are larger than the combined total
of all the clearing-house cities west of the
Rocky Mountains and more than five times
as great as the total of all the Puget Sound
cities combined.
The reports showed that a large
amount of work was done last year
by the chamber and that many top
ics were considered and action taken
thereon.
At the close of the annual meeting
the newly elected board of trustees
met for the purpose of organizing, Mr.
Newhall being in the chair. E. Scott
was re-elected secretary, D*easurer
and librarian. The standing commit
tees for the year were annbunced. It
was the sense of the board that a
communication be sent to William M.
Bunker in Washington requesting him
to confer with the California delega
tion to secure the use of a revenue
cutter for service among the codflsh
ing fleet in Bering Sea.
Fireman's Fund Elects Directors.
The annual meeting of the Fire
man's Fund Insurance Company was
held yesterday. The old board of di
rectors was re-elected. The directors
are William J. Dutton, F. W. Lougee.
John H. Gardiner, Thomas S. Chard,
John T. Wright, W. H. Brown. J. C.
Coleman, Charles R. Bishop, Arthur
A. Smith, John Bermingham, Bernard
Faymonville. The directors will meet
td-day and elect officers, who will be
the old board. An annual statement
was made, which showed that on Jan
uary 1, 1905, the assets of the com
pany amounted to $6,526,439 and
there was a net surplus of $2,233,911.

Negro Attorney Admitted.
Thomas Pearson, a negro attorney
of Oakland, was admitted to practice
yesterday ln the United States District
Court. He is the first negro who has
been accorded this privilege. His
sponsors were United States Customs
Collector Frederick S. Stratton and
William H. Chickerlng. Mr. Pearson
is a man of education and ability.

Captains Arm Swings True
While harboring more than an averÂ»
age allotment of the tender regard for
femininity that is such a beautiful
trait of sea-going character, Mate Al
fred Howard of the trim schooner An
nie Smale believed there should be a
denned limit to woman's prerogatives
and privileges on shipboard. Hoping
that his attitude would not be looked
upon as unchivalrous he desired to
opine that St. Paul's idea anent the
silencing of the gentler sex In churches
might profitably be applied on vessels.
Idealization of woman was all right
in the abstract, but it should not be
allowed to interfere with the mainten
ance of discipline on any craft. In
deed if petticoats were allowed aboard
at allâand, to be candid, he believed
they should notâany effort on their
part to participate in the working of
the ship should be severely reproved.
He had hinted as much to Captain Ed
ward Wirschuliet on more than one
occasion when the better half of that
mariner evinced disposition to usurp
command of the Annie Smale, and if
the skipper had then harkened to him
the incident of the coffee pot might
never have happened.
Very little of Mate Howard's head
was visible as he thus prefaced his
actual complaint in the court of Po
lice Judge Fritz. Bandages covered all
except one eye, a portion of one cheek
and his mouth. The remainder of his
countenance had been parboiled by cot
fee and was quickly anointed with lini
ment. He was seated at breakfast last
Sunday morning with Captain and
Mrs. Wirschuliet in the Annie Smale's
cabin, he testified, and he aod the
skipper were discussing the advisabil
ity of showing the schooner's respect
for the Sabbath 'by hoisting a flag at
her forepeak. It was a subject which
Mrs. Wirschuliet had no right to dis
cuss, as her prescribed duties were
confined to bossing the cook and cabin
boy and keeping her husband's ward
robe in repair. Regardless of that fact
she "butted ln," scouting the mate's
suggestion as to how and where the
flag should be displayed and offering
a most absurd suggestion of her own.
"With due respect to you, missis,"
said the mate, "you will oblige me by
keeping your jaw to yourself. The
skipper and I can settle this question
without any aid from you. So stow
your bloomin' gaff and attend to your
own business."
Then, the mate averred, Mrs. Wir
schuliet sprang to her feet and angrily
slapped his face. He gently pushed
her backward, he further averred,
when her husband picked up the large
pot of boiling coffee and smashed it
upon his head, the effect being a most
severe scalding, to say nothing of the
deep abrasion made by the blow.
Both the captain and his wife, a
tall and angular lady, swore that the
mate struck instead of shoved the lat
ter and that his language to her, prior
to the provocation of the face-slapping,
was too lurid for transcription. The
Judge took the case under advisement
and will have a decision ready ere the
Annie Smale is again prepared to de
part from her moorings at Third and
Berry streets.
Howard Caradoc Rees, the New
York clubman who threatened the
life of R. M. Gignoux at the Hotel
Princeton, resolutely declined to be
escorted to a sanitarium at Belmont,
and will be detained at the Receiving
Hospital until the Lunacy Commission
determine whether he is a fit subject
for the State Insane Asylum.
It was E. D. Roberts, a San Ber
nardino banker and distant relative of
Rees, who proposed the removal to a
sanitarium and announced willingness
to defray all the incidental expense.
When his offer was declined he with
drew from the case, as did Attorney
Oscar Cooper, who had been retained
to protect the defendant's interests.
The warrant alleging Insanity was
sworn to by Gignoux, who fears for his
life at the hands of the clearly de
mented prisoner.
"If any one attempts to remove me
to a sanitarium," Rees informed Rob
erts, "there will be a flght. I must
have liberty or nothing."
But Judge Mogan* believes it would
be unsafe to give the man liberty while
his reasoning is awry.

It was in the guise of a miner from
Tonopah that Policeman McManus in
veigled the Misses Louise Fitzgerald
and Florence Collins into the indiscre
tion of serving him with intoxicating
liquor and permitting him to pay for
it in their apartments on Ellis street.
He looked and acted like the tradi
tional man from the mines, whose sole
aim in life is to separate himself from
his money as expeditiously as possible,
and the ladles were never so astound
ed in all their born days as when he
disclosed his real character and mis
sion. they advanced the
technical plea that inasmuch as each
of them bought drinks after he had
treated them to beer, the gathering
was a social party and its participants
were therefore legally immune from
arrest for violation of the liquor laws.
Judge Mogan gave himself one week
to consult authorities relative to the
efficacy of the plea.
In different guise, that of a sailor,
also yearning to impoverish himself,
the same crafty McManus induced the
Misses Agnes Rockwell and Alma
Scott to temporarily transform their
apartments on O'Farrell street into a
barroom. So nautical were his make
up and his manner that neither Miss
Rockwell nor Miss Scott hesitated to
obey when he demanded to be served
with "a little drink." Miss Rockwell
told the desk sergeant that she al
most had a cat fit when their visitor
stood revealed ln his true colors, and
Miss Scott informed the same author
ity that anyone could have knocked
her down with a straw when McMan
us flashed his star. They will tell
Judge Mogan more about it to-mor
row.
So varied and ingenious and decep
tive are the disguises in which Mc-
Manus prowls the tenderloin in quest
of "blind pigs" that the Illegal traf
fic is greatly Impeded by the fear of
its conductors that they may be taken
in and done for by him. Indeed, some
of the more cautious lawbreakers in
sist on being presented with a certif
icate of identity ere they consent to
sell "a little drink" to any one. In
the language of an admiring brother
policeman, "McManus has 'em buffa
loed."

"Theo Russell, a woman," is the
way it is written on Judge Mogan's
calendar, because Bailiffs Hickey and
Mahoney scoured the corridors of the
Hall of Justice in vain effort to find
Theo Russell, a man. The charge was
vagrancy and the defendant was at
large on ball.
"We can't find him," said Bailiff
Hickey breathlessly to the bench, and
the Judge was about to order the bail
forfeited when a flashily attired young
woman arose from among the spec
tators and proclaimed herself Theo
Russell. So taken aback was the court
that it continued the case till next
week in order to give itself time to
recover from the shock.
Patrick Daly, night watchman at
the Hibernia Brewery, 1241 Howard
street reported to the police yester
day that six men attempted to break
into the brewery about 8 o'clock on
Sunday morning. He saw them from
a fire escape, where he happened to
be at the time. He was unable to give
a description of the men except that
each was over six feet tall. >

NEW OFFICIALS
ARE SELECTED
Dr. Sidney Worth to Be Put
on Health Board and W. H.
Met son on Park Commission
THE MAYOR'S CHOICES
Several Candidates in Line
for Three Secretaryships
and Other Minor Positions
Mayor Schmitz did not put in an ap
pearance yesterday at his office, con
sequently the official announcement of
the appointments to the vacancies on
the Health and Park boards were de
layed, although the selection of Dr.
Sidney Worth and William H. Met
son, respectively, to the boards named
has been decided upon. Dr. Worth's
appointment was foreshadowed in last
Sunday's Call, while the fact that Met
son would be named on the Park Com
| mission has been frequently mentioned
ln these columns.
Dr. Worth owes his appointment to
Dr. James W. Ward, president of the
Health Board, to whom the selection
was left by the Mayor. Worth is a
graduate of the class of 1874 of the
Homeopathic Medical College of New
York and is a distinguished exponent
of the school, of which Dr. Ward is
also a prominent member.
Metson was really the only candidate
ever seriously considered for Park
Commissioner. Opposition to his ap
pointment came from certain quarters,
which, however, gradually simmered
down to a half hearted support, and
gave the Mayor the opportunity to de
cide in his favor. Metsorf's appoint
ment is considered as a rebuke to the
Phelan administration, as Metson was
the only Psrk Commissioner not re
appointed when the charter went into
effect in 1900. v
There have been no new develop
ments in the proposed "shift" of cer
tain city officials whereby Supervisor
Finn is to be transferred to the Fire
Commission in order that the Mayor
may name some man who can effective
ly champion his cause in the Board of
Supervisors against the McNab fac
tion. It is said that Fire Commissioner
Boyne will receive a more comfortable
billet to make way for Finn.
The appointments to the Police, Park
and Works boards and the storekeeper
ship of the Board of Education have
not been settled. James de Succa, who
has beesr mentioned for the storekeep
ership, may be made secretary of the
i [Board of Works, while Herbert
Schmltx, ithis said, will become for the
. second time secretary of the Park
j Commission. For the other places Bar
i ney Flood, John Fitzgerald and George
i Berger are being considered.
WORKS BOARD MAY CANCEL
STREET REPAIR CONTRACT
Commissioner Aigeltinger to Present
Resolution So Work May Be
Done by the City.
Commissioner of Works Aigeltinger
decided yesterday to prepare a reso
lution for presentation to the Board
of Works at its meeting to-day declar
ing the contract of the F. M. Torke
Company for repairing bituminous
streets canceled in the event that the
company refuses to continue the work.
Algeltinger stated that he. would at
once enter into arrangements where
by the Board of Works itself will un
dertake the task of repairing the
numerous chuckholes in the streets by
day labor.
The company has promised to file
an answer to-day as to whether It will
agree to resume the repairs. In al!
likelihood the answer will be in the
negative, as the members of the com
pany have expressed themselves to
that effect.
ONE PARENT COMPLAlNS.âSuperintend
ent Langdon stated yesterday that one parent
had complained of the transfer of the primary
classes of the James Lick School to the Noe
Valley School, because it necessitated her child
walking a few blocks more. Other complaints
are expected, but Langdon says the complaint
la without merit as the two schools are only
four blocks and a half apart.

DISCOVERS MAN
UNDER HIS BED
H. Bobinson, Musician From
Portland, Has Peculiar Ex
perience on His Arrival
H. Robinson, a musician from Port
land, obtained a warrant from Police
Judge Mogan yesterday for the arrest
of "John Doe" Kline on a charge of
burglary. Robinson says he arrived in
the city on January 7. His wife pre
ceded him.
Late in the afternoon Robinson went
to the ferry to get his grips, and
dropped into a saloon to have a drink.
While chatting with the bartender
Kline came in and joined in the con
versation. Robinson remarked that
his grips were heavy, and Kline offered
to help him carry them. They got the
grips and took them to the United
States Hotel on Eddy street. Robinson
says he could not get rid of Kline for
some hours, but finally he gave him
the slip in-a saloon.
About an hour later Robinson went,
to his room and discovered Kline un :
der the bed. He ordered him out of
the room. Next morning, while Rob
inson was dressing, he discovered that
his watch and chain and $250 in coin
had been stolen from his pockets. He
came to the conclusion that Kline
had returned when he was asleep,
crawled through the transom and
stole his property.
Robinson says he did not report the
burglary earlier because he was ad
vised to consult an attorney with the
object of recovering his loss from the
proprietor of the hotel owing to the
defective transom, which" had no glass
in it.
MURDERER OF RANCHER IS
BROUGHT FROM HOLLISTER
Richard Maligna, Who Shot and
Killed Charles Zanders, to Be
Tried at Merced.
Richard Maughs. who shot and
killed Charles Zanders, a young
rancher in Merced County on Sun
day, was brought from Holister to the
Cltv Prison here yesterday morning by
Under Sheriff W. O. Moore. In the
afternoon Moore left with his prisoner
for Merced.
According to Moore the murder was
a cold-blooded one. Maughs was cook
at the ranch and had also to look af
ter the fences. On Sunday Zanders
checked him for allowing some cattle
to stray through a broken fence and
Maughs shot him dead while they
were within a few feet of each other.
Edward Cole, Zanders' partner, and a
ranch hand were close by at the time.
Maughs armed himself with a shot
gun and revolver and left the ranch.
Cole rode into Hollister and notified
Sheriff Croxon of San Benito County,
who wired to Moore. Moore and an
armed posse started out to search for
the murderer and learned that he had
returned and surrendered himself to
Hugh French, owner of an adjoining
ranch, who took him to Hollister.
â¢ Maughs told Moore that it was his
original intention to put up a fight
and if he saw ,that. his capture was
Inevitable he was to commit suicide.
He was brought round this way be
cause Moore said It was an easier
Journey than driving seventy-five
miles by road from Hollister.

on the installment plan and paid a

pawned Â»the ring for $115.
